throughout the division and provide general support to all units in the division or brigade support areas. These
teams provide disbursing and payment support in their area of operations. When mission variables dictate,
the FST can be augmented with additional support from the finance platoon. All accountable items are cleared
through the disbursing section of the C-FICO.

### SECTION V – TACTICAL LEVEL OF WARFARE

2-122. The tactical level of warfare is the level of warfare at which forces plan and execute battles and
engagements to achieve military objectives (JP 3-0). Activities at this level focus on tactics—the
employment, ordered arrangement, and directed actions of forces in relation to each other (ADP 3-90).
Operational-level headquarters determine objectives and provide resources for tactical operations. Tactical-
level commanders plan and execute operations to include battles, engagements, and small-unit actions.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**35**
**Chapter 2**
2-123. The strategic and operational levels of warfare provide the context for tactical operations. Without
this context, tactical operations devolve into a series of disconnected and unfocused actions. Likewise,
tactical operations inform strategic and operational objectives, ensuring that those objectives remain tethered
to reality and adjust, when necessary, according to the situation. Strategic and operational success is a
measure of how the achievement of objectives in one or more battles contributes to winning a major operation
or campaign. (See ADP 3-90 for more information on tactics.)
2-124. The following paragraphs discuss FM at the tactical level of warfare. Knowing the capabilities and
organizational structure of FM elements at the tactical level of warfare is essential to planning, preparing,
executing, and assessing FM operations.
2-125. Figure 2-12 illustrates the FM organizations and command staff structures at the tactical level of
warfare.
**Figure 2-12. Army FM organizations at the tactical level of warfare**

## DIVISION

2-126. The division is the Army's principal tactical warfighting formation during large-scale combat
operations. Its primary role is to serve as a tactical headquarters commanding brigades. A division conducts
operations in an area assigned by its higher headquarters—normally a corps. It task-organizes its subordinate
forces according to mission variables. A division typically commands between two and five maneuver
brigades, a mix of functional and multifunctional brigades, and a variety of smaller enabling units. The
division is typically the lowest tactical echelon that employs capabilities from multiple domains to achieve
convergence during large-scale combat operations. The division's primary purpose is to win battles and
engagements. During limited contingencies, it can organize itself to serve in multiple roles. The division
sustainment brigade coordinates for finance support to units within the division's area of operations. See FM
3-94 and ATP 3-91 for more information about Army divisions.
2-127. The roles of the division include acting as a—
•
Tactical headquarters.
**36**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Financial Management at Echelon**
•
ARFOR headquarters (with significant augmentation).
•
CFLCC (with significant augmentation).
•
JTF headquarters (with significant augmentation).
2-128. The division G-8 is the principal staff officer responsible for all RM functions in support of the
division commander's priorities, as well as funding for assigned forces. The G-8 coordinates, synchronizes,
and funds validated requirements and identifies appropriate funding sources. The division G-8’s primary
responsibilities are providing advice and guidance concerning resources and ensuring that adequate funding
sources are available for the division commander, the division staff, and subordinate units. The division G-8
is the primary interface and technical expert for brigade S-8s.
2-129. The division G-8 has a critical role in operational contract support and maintains a close relationship
with supporting contracting elements. The G-8 coordinates contracting support to ensure legality, prompt
payment, and reconciliation of contract payments to identify unused funds.
2-130. The division G-8's functions include—
•
Identifying sources of funds, receiving funding, and distributing and controlling funds to
subordinate units.
•
Tracking costs and obligations.
•
Providing accounting support.
•
Establishing and implementing internal controls.
•
Contributing to annexes, running estimates, orders, and the concept of support.

## BRIGADE

2-131. Brigades include maneuver brigades, multifunctional brigades, and functional brigades. Maneuver
brigades are the Army's primary combined arms, close-operations force and principal ground maneuver units
of the division. They have organic maneuver and sustainment battalions. The three types of maneuver
brigades are armored, infantry, and Stryker. Maneuver brigades normally operate as part of a division.
2-132. There is a wide range of multifunctional support brigades. These brigades provide a variety of
functions in support of operations. Normally attached to a corps or division, these brigades may be under the
command of a joint or multinational headquarters. Multifunctional support brigades include the sustainment
brigade, combat aviation brigade, division artillery, transportation brigade expeditionary, and maneuver
enhancement brigade.
2-133. Functional brigades or groups provide a single function or capability. These brigades can provide
support for a theater, corps, or division, depending on how each is tailored. Functional brigades include the
air and missile defense brigade, civil affairs brigade, engineer brigade, expeditionary military intelligence
brigade, signal brigade, military police brigade, medical brigade, and theater tactical signal brigade.
2-134. The brigade S-8 conducts RM operations and serves as the primary RM advisor to the brigade
commander. The brigade S-8 is the RM expert on the brigade commander's principal staff, responsible for
providing timely, accurate, and relevant comptroller advice to the brigade commander and staff.
2-135. The brigade S-8's functions include—
•
Identifying sources of funds, receiving funding, certifying funds, and controlling funds.
•
Tracking costs and obligations.
•
Providing accounting support.
•
Establishing and implementing internal controls.
•
Contributing to annexes, running estimates, orders, and the concept of support.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**37**

## This page intentionally left blank.

### Chapter 3

## Finance Operations

Finance operations support the sustainment of Army, joint, and multinational operations
through the execution of disbursing, banking, payment support, accounting, internal controls,
and financial data analytics following policies and guidelines established by national FM
providers. Effective finance operations extend the Army’s operational reach as well as increase
relative combat power, enabling commanders to prevail in large-scale combat operations.

### SECTION I – FINANCE OPERATIONS IN THEATER

3-1. Finance operations are externally focused, integrating and synchronizing national provider (including
the U.S. Treasury, DFAS, and Federal Reserve Bank) resources with tactical finance units to manage
banking, disbursing, and pay support. This section discusses finance operations in support of setting the
theater, theater opening, and theater closing.

## SET THE THEATER

3-2. As part of finance operations in mobilization and pre-deployment, to assist with setting the theater,
finance planners should review operation plans and prepare the finance appendix of the OPORD to develop
the FM concept of support. Some of the actions for finance planners to consider during the planning phase
are—
•
Consolidate and analyze the economic assessment for the area of operations.
•
Provide financial advice and assistance.
•
Determine if foreign currencies are to be used and their exchange rates.
•
Develop requirements for check cashing, emergency payments, currency conversion, funding of
paying agents, foreign currency conversion, solatium payments, recording of cost data, travel,
civilian pay, and funds disbursement.
•
Verify the entitlements determination of hostile fire pay, imminent danger pay, hardship duty pay,
family separation allowance, special leave accrual, and combat zone tax exclusion.
•
Determine appropriate check cashing limits and recommend approval by the commander.
•
Coordinate with the DFAS crisis coordination center.
•
Identify and coordinate collaboration and strategy between the members of the fiscal triad.
•
Coordinate with USAFMCOM for ecommerce requirements.
•
Determine accounting and central funding support needed for the operation.
•
Determine banking laws, regulations, and banking support requirements.
•
Determine appropriate quantities of foreign currencies and formalize resupply procedures.
•
Support noncombatant evacuation operations.
•
Identify requirements to provide support to other U.S. and multinational organizations in the area
of operations.
•
Determine the requirement for LDAs.
•
Coordinate with the staff judge advocate (SJA) to ensure legal considerations are reviewed.
•
When possible, FIBN, FICO, and sustainment brigade leaders should conduct pre-deployment
coordination (preferably face-to-face) to begin building essential working relationships and prepare
to integrate the finance units and finance personnel that will follow on into theater.
•
Coordinate with signal support assets to ensure adequate bandwidth and other communication
support is sufficient to support FMIS.
3-3. During competition, finance operations focus on key activities that establish appropriate levels of
support to resource critical requirements to enable the commander in setting the theater, executing military
engagements, facilitating security cooperation across the AOR, and funding combined training exercises.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**39**
**Chapter 3**

## THEATER OPENING

3-4. Based on operational variables, USAFMCOM deploys an AFMC team to provide initial theater opening
activities comprised of central funding operations, establishing and troubleshooting FMIS, and establishing
and executing internal controls.
3-5. During theater opening and setting the theater, the AFMC and FIOC provide early entry capability to
plan and execute disbursing operations, banking operations, and payment support. In addition, the FIOC
continues to refine plans through staff estimates while the AFMC continues to improve finance infrastructure
to include disbursing, system integration, and internal controls.
3-6. When applicable, the theater opening TSC, ESC, or sustainment brigade will receive its complementary
theater finance capability to augment the theater opening mission.

### Theater Opening – Operation Talisman Sabre

In 2022, the INDOPACOM commander determined it was necessary to conduct an
exercise to plan and rehearse opening the theater in the event of crisis or conflict. The
INDOPACOM Pacific Pathways Exercise Program selected “Operation Talisman
Sabre” to rehearse the concept.
2023 was the first time Talisman Sabre focused on theater opening. As the plan
matured, logistics planners quickly realized that there was a critical missing element to
make the concept successful, the Army Finance and Comptroller infrastructure. In
February 2023, the USARPAC G-8 and the 175th Finance Operations Center received
the mission to plan and emplace the FM infrastructure required to successfully conduct
the operation.
The 175th operations section quickly determined three theater capabilities were
needed: central funding, internal control oversight, and finance systems. At the time,
these capabilities existed as individual sections to perform those specific roles;
however, they now reside as sections within the 45th AFMC as teams assigned to the
theater. Each section conducted mission analysis to provide the required support to
the 8th TSC, the 593rd ESC, and the 25th Infantry Division sustainment brigade.
The theater opening focus of each capability demonstrated the need to rehearse their
functions. The central funding section identified that cash would need to be procured
from the Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco and flown into theater utilizing Travis
Air Force Base, located approximately 80 miles from the bank. The finance systems
section identified the need to network all finance forces across three logistics hubs.
The internal control section focused on plans to evaluate field disbursing and
commercial vendor pay sites. The 175th operations section focused on ensuring all
capabilities were synchronized and nested with 8th TSC, the 593rd ESC, and the 25th
Infantry Division Sustainment Brigade as well as with host-nation partners. The 175th
operations section also coordinated with G-8s from across echelons to synchronize
and deconflict the entire procure-to-pay process, while simultaneously coordinating the
deployment of forward RM personnel for each command.

## THEATER CLOSING

3-7. As part of finance operations during redeployment and demobilization operations, finance units
should—
•
Reconcile all finance transactions.
•
Further reduce cash in theater.
•
Close out the accounts of paying agents.
**40**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Finance Operations**
•
Ensure Service member deployment-related entitlements are stopped.
•
Audit pay accounts.
•
Convert from contingency accounting processing codes back to home station accounting processing
codes.
•
Close LDAs.
•
Deactivate DSSNs.
3-8. To eliminate the unnecessary efforts of tracking and reporting monthly financial and checking accounts
during the period of inactive usage, the FISC disbursing officer, in coordination with USAFMCOM, can
request to deactivate or close a DSSN through the U.S. Department of Treasury. The FISC disbursing officer
must conduct mandated procedures throughout the proper channels in order to deactivate or close the specific
DSSN. Some of the basic procedures are—
•
Deposit all currency, coin, and negotiable instruments.
•
Close LDAs and properly dispose of all undeliverable checks (if applicable).
•
Submit the memorandum that is required to inactivate or close a DSSN.
•
Retain blank U.S. Treasury check stock to use if disbursing operations are reactivated.
•
Account for assets on hand. This includes accounting for deficiencies and shortages such as
dishonored checks or losses of funds.
3-9. If required to reopen a DSSN, the disbursing officer has the authority to do so by submitting the report
of commencement of disbursing operations. However, if any cash or negotiable instruments are on hand, the
disbursing officer cannot temporarily close a DSSN. See DOD 7000.14-R Volume 5 for complete procedures
for deactivating or closing a DSSN.

### SECTION II – DISBURSING

3-10. Disbursing is a critical function within finance operations. It ensures all payments are made in
accordance with DOD regulations and assures the accuracy of documentation and timely payment of goods
and services.

## OVERVIEW OF DISBURSING SUPPORT OPERATIONS

3-11. Disbursing sections make payments (currency, electronic funds transfer [EFT], stored value cards,
checks) on properly prepared and certified vouchers, receive collections, receive and control currencies, and
maintain accountable records. They also cash negotiable instruments, manage established LDAs, make
foreign currency conversions, and determine the need for currency and its replenishment.
3-12. Disbursing support is comprised of currency management and support to contingency operations cost.
Some of disbursing support major tasks include—
•
Establishing and closing a DSSN.
•
Establishing and maintaining theater cash holding authorities.
•
Providing cash management/currency support.
•
Providing reporting requirements.
•
Opening, maintaining, and closing an LDA.
•
Providing procurement support.
•
Making special payments.
•
Performing personal cash services support.
3-13. Disbursements are cash, check, EFT, U.S. debit card, Intra-Governmental Payment and Collection
System, or interfund payments that liquidate established obligations, disburse amounts previously collected
into a deposit fund account, or provide payment in advance of performance. The disbursing office ensures
that only transactions that have been certified for payment by certifying officials are processed for payment.
The disbursing officer is a military member designated to disburse monies and render accounts according to
laws and regulations governing the disbursement of public monies. Disbursing officers safeguard public
funds they collect or otherwise have in their custody or control, and they may have pecuniary liability for the
loss of these funds. Disbursing officers will not lend, use, deposit in banks, or exchange public funds in their
possession for other funds except as specifically allowed by law. They secure public funds until ordered by
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**41**
**Chapter 3**
proper authority to transfer or pay out the funds. When disbursing officers receive orders for transfer or
payment, they faithfully and promptly execute the directed transactions and perform all other duties as fiscal
agents of the U.S. Treasury imposed by law or regulations.

## DISBURSING ROLES BY ECHELON

3-14. The U.S. Treasury appoints specific accountable agents to disbursing activities. DOD 7000.14-R
Volume 5 defines these key positions and accountable agents such as disbursing officers, deputy disbursing
officers, disbursing agents, and cashiers. For additional policies and responsibilities for accountable agents,
see DOD 7000.14-R Volume 5.

### ARMYFINANCIALMANAGEMENTCENTER

3-15. During theater opening and setting the theater, the AFMC team conducts procurement and disbursing
operations through the establishment of central funding until the FISC arrives. The AFMC director is the
Army's senior disbursing officer and primary liaison with DFAS and the U.S. Treasury. This individual is
responsible for central funding, reviewing qualification of all DSSN account holders, and recommending
approval of disbursing officer appointments to the USAFMCOM commander.

### FINANCESUPPORTCENTER

3-16. The FISC serves as the conduit for banking and disbursing operations between the theater of operations,
national providers, and finance elements in theater. Generally, the FISC coordinates with USAFMCOM,
national providers, and finance units for the establishment of LDAs, establishes a central funding capability,
and publishes the theater's finance operation's policies and procedures. These policies must be nested with
theater OPORDs and promulgated throughout the AOR. This ensures consistent execution of theater policies
across the theater. The FISC ensures that theater disbursing support is synchronized at all levels. Additionally,
the FISC coordinates closely with the contracting support brigade to ensure that theater finance and
contracting are well-planned and synchronized within theater.
3-17. The FISC works closely with the TSC or ESC to ensure that cash on hand meets the commander's
requirements. The AFMC team provides the FISC with immediate notification of changing cash requirements
and policies. In coordination with the AFMC team, the FISC establishes reporting procedures to monitor
currency usage throughout theater.
3-18. The FISC director, in coordination with the AFMC, recommends policies for the TSC commander and
procedures regarding currency use on local and partial payments and check cashing amounts. However, the
CCDR retains final authority on these policies and procedures and may establish controls on the amounts of
U.S. and foreign currency available and the type of currency used. This is necessary to control black market
activities, secure the money of individual Service members, and help control problems related to currency
inflation of either U.S. or HN currency. If the Army is appointed as the executive agent, the FISC serves as
the theater central funding proponent for all U.S. branches of Service in theater.

### THEATER/CORPSFINANCEBATTALIONS

3-19. FIBNs have two structures: one for the theater (the T-FIBN) and one for the corps (the C-FIBN).
T-FIBNs are exclusively Reserve Component organizations and C-FIBNs belong exclusively to the Active
Component.
3-20. FIBNs receive funding for their operations from the FISC. In cases where the FISC is absent from
theater, the AFMC team or FIBN assumes central funding responsibilities. As the theater matures and a FISC
deploys, the AFMC team or FIBN transfers its central funding responsibilities to the arriving FISC. The
FIBN provides the FISC with estimates of currency requirements weekly. Once funded, the disbursing officer
maintains full accountability of funds entrusted to them. The disbursing section, which is managed by the
deputy disbursing officer/disbursing agent maintains the day-to-day execution of disbursing operations for
the FIBN. The FIBN is responsible for funding its subordinate companies and ensuring they have procedures
in place for daily account balancing.
3-21. The FIBN deploys its subordinate companies to provide task-organized support for disbursing
operations. It coordinates with the FISC for the placement of FICOs to provide effective theater FM support.
**42**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Finance Operations**
It should also coordinate closely with the contracting support battalion to ensure financial coordination and
advice is addressed when letting contracts.

### THEATER/CORPSFINANCECOMPANIES

3-22. FICOs at both the theater and corps level provide disbursing support to subordinate FSTs and paying
agents. While assigned to their respective T-FIBNs and C-FIBNs, FICOs generally conduct disbursing
support autonomously from their parent FIBNs during operations in the area of operations. As such, FICOs
must be prepared to conduct a full range of finance support missions while geographically separated from
their parent units. They will require the necessary expertise in manual and automated disbursing operations
as well as ecommerce operations in their area of operations. Depending on the mission of the FICO, the
disbursing officer will appoint disbursing agents to manage and execute disbursing operations.
3-23. To facilitate and effectively execute contract payments, FICOs are normally co-located with supporting
contracting battalions or detachments. It is critical that FICO commanders synchronize finance support within
their area of operations.

### DISBURSINGSECTIONS

3-24. Within finance organizations, the disbursing section serves as the central funding element, providing
cash management currency support. It also maintains inventory of and distributes ecommerce hardware (for
example, EagleCash cards, laptops, kiosks, and point of sale devices).
**Currency Found in Personal Effects**
3-25. Appointed officials inventory all U.S. and foreign currency (paper, coin, precious metals) found among
the personal effects of Soldiers and civilians captured, killed, or medically evacuated. These funds must be
turned in to the nearest finance unit. These funds are either taken into the disbursing officer’s custody and
accountability and placed in a suspense deposit account or exchanged for a U.S. Treasury check.
**Savings Deposit Program**
3-26. Military members deployed to combat zones, qualified hazardous duty areas, or certain contingency
operations as designated by the Secretary of Defense may be eligible to deposit all or part of their unallotted
pay into a DOD savings account up to $10,000 during a single deployment. Interest accrues on the account
at an annual rate of 10% compounded quarterly. Soldiers desiring to participate in this program can do so by
cash deposit with the servicing unit or by allotment. See DOD 7000.14-R Volume 7A for eligibility,
limitations, and more information on the savings deposit program.
**Check Cashing (Accommodation Exchange)**
3-27. The negotiable instruments that a disbursing officer is authorized to cash (payable in U.S. currency)
include—
•
U.S. Treasury Checks.
•
Traveler's checks.
•
Money orders.
•
Third-party checks (a check on which the drawer, the drawee, and the payee are all different parties).
•
State and local government checks.
•
Credit card checks.
•
Business checks.
•
Personal checks.
3-28. The payee must be eligible for check cashing services. The disbursing officer has the right to deny
check cashing privileges to anyone based on the non-availability of cash or personnel resources. The
AFMC/FISC develops and recommends theater policy establishing the check cashing amount thresholds for
authorized personnel to the theater Army G-8. All authorized personnel who request check cashing privileges
shall consent, in writing, to immediate collection against their pay for the total of any dishonored check. The
use of stored value card technology should be the primary method to provide authorized personnel with
access to their funds. The AFMC/FISC develops and recommends to the theater Army G-8 theater policy on
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**43**
**Chapter 3**
local and partial payment limits and frequency for authorized personnel. See DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 for
more information.
**Advance Pay and Allowances**
3-29. The purpose of an advance of pay is to ease hardships imposed by the lack of regular payments when
Soldiers are mobilized, ordered to duty at distant stations, or deployed for more than 30 days to locations
where they do not have means of withdrawing funds from their personal bank accounts. The use of stored
value card technology should be the primary method to provide authorized personnel with access to their
funds. See DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 7A for more information.
**Disbursing Support to Noncombatant Evacuation Operations**
3-30. A noncombatant evacuation operation is the withdrawal of civilians and noncombatants from a
threatened area abroad that is carried out with the assistance of the Department of Defense (JP 3-68). These
operations are conducted to assist the DOS in evacuating U.S. citizens, DOD civilian personnel, and
designated HN and third-country nationals whose lives are in danger from locations in a foreign nation to an
appropriate safe haven. Although normally considered in connection with hostile action, evacuation may also
be conducted in anticipation of, or in response to, any natural or manmade disaster. During noncombatant
evacuation operations, the U.S. ambassador is ultimately responsible for the successful completion of the
operation and the safety of the evacuees. In support of a noncombatant evacuation operation, finance units
make advances of pay, dislocation allowance payments, and travel entitlement pay. Evacuation payments
would be made at the designated safe haven. If the situation warrants, minimum payments may be made in
theater, and this would preclude an outflow of U.S. currency in the theater of operation.

## DISBURSING STATION MANAGEMENT

3-31. The FISC and each FIBN may deploy with a contingency DSSN. The DSSN is assigned to a disbursing
officer by Treasury and indicates authority to receive and disburse public funds and issue checks on the
Treasury's behalf. The accountholders (for example, the disbursing officer) within the FISC and FIBN are
accountable for the funds within their respective areas of operations. The FISC provides currency to the
FIBNs to support finance operations. This decentralized approach allows for greater accountability and direct
oversight of cash. See DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 for more information on disbursing policy and procedures.
3-32. The disbursing officer, deputy disbursing officer, and disbursing agent must be nominated by a
commander and appointed by DFAS to maintain the DSSN. The establishment of the DSSN is requested
through DFAS. Treasury approval must also be requested through DFAS. Once the activation letter is
submitted to DFAS, the following must occur:
•
Order initial U.S. Treasury checks.
•
Obtain employee identification number.
•
Establish routing transit number.
•
Identify and access systems (for example, Over the Counter Channel application [OTCnet] and
International Treasury Services [ITS.gov]).
•
Acquire/transfer Computerized Accounts Payable System-Windows test files to ITS.gov prior to
going live.
•
Obtain user identification for all systems.
•
Establish cost centers.
•
Establish suspense account for DSSN.
•
Reroute analysis unmatched transactions, suspense account reconciliation.
•
Establish Army disbursing symbol number, Defense Military Pay Office, and company code.
•
Establish a new fiscal station number (if required).

## CASH HOLDING AUTHORITY

3-33. Disbursing officers are responsible for currency support. These operations include acquiring,
accounting, and safeguarding currency and U.S. Treasury checks; collecting and redistributing surplus
currency; and disposing of mutilated and contaminated currency. The disbursing officer requests to hold cash
**44**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Finance Operations**
currency at personal risk from the appropriate approving authority, which authorizes the limit of cash a
disbursing officer can have or must have to execute currency operations. The cash holding authority is used
to make miscellaneous cash payments, conduct exchanges, and other specifically authorized transactions.
The approving authority ensures the amount of the request is within established guidelines and that
management controls exist to ensure that routine reviews of cash requirements are conducted. When special
circumstances require disbursing officers to increase their cash holding authority beyond stated amounts, the
approving authority may authorize holding amounts temporarily in excess of the limits based on the
circumstances. See DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 for additional information on cash holding authority.
3-34. Cash holding authority limits are established by—
•
Requesting cash holding authority.
•
Coordinating with supporting units to determine currency requirements.
•
Determining the number of customers and the funding requirements from unified action partners.
•
Utilizing the cash holding template to determine the type (U.S. dollars and foreign currency) and
amount of cash.

## CASH MANAGEMENT/CURRENCY SUPPORT

3-35. Cash management/currency support is the act of supplying U.S. currency, foreign currencies, U.S.
Treasury and local currency checks, available ecommerce (for example, EagleCash Cards, Over the Counter-
Net, government purchase cards, U.S. debit cards, and mobile payments) and, in some operations, precious
metals (gold, silver) to U.S. and multinational forces in the operational area. Additionally, finance units
provide currency and coins to Army and Air Force Exchange Service facilities, tactical field exchanges,
postal units, and companies serving as in-theater defense contractors. Finance units provide kiosks and point-
of-sale devices that enable EagleCash Card transactions. This service is a form of mobile banking. Mobile
banking is a portable solution that uses mobile phone networks and incorporates existing prepaid debit card,
internet, and mobile phone technology to transmit instructions to financial networks that facilitate funds
transfers (for example, payments) to concession vendors.
3-36. The FISC, in coordination with the AFMC, provides cash management/currency support (U.S. and
foreign) in theater. In coordination with the HN, U.S. banks, credit unions, and military banking facilities
provide currency to the FIBNs and other Services or multinational forces in accordance with interservice and
intergovernmental agreements. The FISC and FIBNs maintain the DSSN and may be designated to provide
currency to all components of a joint or multinational force.

### CASHMANAGEMENTSUPPORTINGTASKS

3-37. The following functions are performed under cash management/currency support:
•
Perform cash acquisition and cost analysis.
•
Perform cash holding authority analysis (U.S. and foreign currency).
•
Document the applicable foreign currency market rates (for example, the **Spot Rate****—the price that**
**is quoted for immediate settlement on a commodity, a security, or a currency).**
•
Determine the process and cost associated with acquisition and delivery of U.S. and local currency.
•
Obtain the budget rate for contracts paid in local currency.
•
Determine the availability of a foreign currency fluctuation account for local currency payments.
•
Determine the history, evaluation, and inflation of U.S. currency in relation to the local currency.
•
Acquire and account for currency (U.S. and foreign currency)/checks.
•
Dispose and report mutilated or damaged currency/checks.
•
Maintain and control agreements that support multinational forces, contractors, and DA Civilians.
•
Account for captured currency.
•
Investigate or request investigation of loss of funds.
•
Receive collections (for example, postal and moral, welfare, and recreation).
•
Perform cash verifications in accordance with DOD 7000.14-R Volume 5.
•
Review stateside bank operating agreement for Annex to support contingency funding requirements.
•
Safeguard currency/checks.
•
Support and facilitate funding and retrograde missions.
•
Enforce finance policies.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**45**
**Chapter 3**
**Note.** All funds must be safeguarded in accordance with DOD 7000.14-R Volume 5.

### BULKCURRENCYMANAGEMENT

3-38. Generally, the FISC provides the primary source of currency in the operational theater. Deployed
FIBNs and sister Service finance organizations will draw and return cash through the FISC. In contingency
operations where there is no FISC deployed, FIBNs must be prepared to resource cash requirements through
the AFMC in support of theater operations. In these instances, pre-deployment coordination with national
providers, agreements with approved funding sources, and establishments of LDAs are critical to support
funding requirements.

### FOREIGNCURRENCY

3-39. The AFMC and FISC must work with the DOS and Treasury Department to determine whether the use
of U.S. currency will be beneficial or detrimental to the HN in conjunction with military/security
requirements in accordance with mission variables. When a foreign currency payment is required, the
preferred method of payment is via EFT using ITS.gov. The establishment of an LDA enables finance to
disburse checks and conduct EFT payments in local currency when the infrastructure or mission does not
support payments made through ITS.gov. The FISC/AFMC banking officer will confer with the local U.S.
Embassy for assistance in determining which banks meet the selection criteria established by the Treasury.
The banking officer will then provide those recommendations to the disbursing officer to establish an LDA.
3-40. Once the disbursing officer selects an LDA, they must formally request approval and designation of
that institution as an authorized LDA from the Treasury. DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 provides specific
guidelines on the establishment of an LDA. LDAs are not limited to the banking section of the FISC/AFMC.
FIBN account holders are encouraged to establish an LDA to facilitate foreign currency cash operations. All
transactions involving foreign currency are treated as cash transactions and follow the same principles and
guidelines established for controlling U.S. currency. All disbursing officers, deputy disbursing officers,
paying agents, and cashiers who engage in foreign currency transactions will maintain a record of such
transactions on a DD Form 2663 (Foreign Currency Control Record). As required for each account, the
disbursing officer will solicit competitive proposals from local financial institutions in the area to secure the
most beneficial banking agreement in conjunction with the FISC/AFMC banking section.

### CURRENCYEXCHANGE

3-41. Finance units maintain an important role in currency exchange and currency control. Currency
exchange plans must consider all members of the uniformed Services, DOD Civilians, authorized U.S.
contractor personnel, other United States Government (USG) personnel, nongovernmental organization
(NGO) personnel, and paying agents. All currency exchanges from U.S. to foreign currency or from foreign
currency to U.S. currency must comply with currency control standards to discourage black market activities.
3-42. Finance units will exercise caution when exchanging U.S. currency to alleviate laundering points for
enemy currency, counterfeit currency, black market operations, and currency arbitrage. EagleCash cards or
U.S. Treasury checks are issued in exchange for excess currency accumulated by Service members.
Additionally, military banking facilities, tactical field exchanges, and non-appropriated funds custodians may
need to deposit their holdings of currency and coins with the nearest finance unit.

### MULTINATIONALFORCESCURRENCYSUPPORT

3-43. When an agreement has been negotiated between the U.S. and a foreign nation or multinational
organization, disbursing officers may be authorized, on an emergency basis, to advance funds to cashiers or
members of the multinational force. Such agreements may require multinational forces to provide reciprocal
support to U.S. forces. Finance commanders must be aware of agreements with allies or HNs, which include
reciprocal financial support if authorized. The FISC director or account holder is responsible for obtaining
copies of these agreements and disseminating them to lower elements. These agreements will be coordinated
with the DOS and SJA for legal sufficiency prior to implementation. See DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 for
additional information.
**46**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Finance Operations**

### CAPTURED ANDCOUNTERFEITCURRENCYANALYSIS

3-44. Procedures must be in place to address the handling of captured, worn, or mutilated currency. Any
disbursing officer acquiring captured foreign currency must ensure the funds are safeguarded and delivered
to the Treasury. Captured foreign currency shall not be collected into the disbursing officer’s accountability.
Counterfeit currency or coinage accepted in conjunction with official disbursing office business transactions
are processed as physical losses of funds. See DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 for additional information.

## DISBURSING OFFICER SUPPORT TO PAYING AGENTS

3-45. The disbursing officer or designee briefs new paying agents to inform them of their responsibilities and
provides them detailed written instructions regarding the specific functions they are to perform, procedures
for the safeguarding and expeditious return of currency, and other instructions deemed necessary. Although
requesting unit commanders appoint paying agents (by orders), disbursing officers or their designees retain
the right to refuse funding paying agents because of their inherent duty to properly safeguard government
funds.

### PAYINGAGENTSUPPORT

3-46. A paying agent is a U.S. military member or DOD civilian employee appointed by the commander to
act as an agent of a disbursing officer. The purpose of a paying agent is to make specific payments, currency
conversions, or check cashing transactions from funds temporarily advanced to the agent by the disbursing
agent. Paying agents are individuals whose regular duties do not involve disbursing functions and who are
not organizationally located in the disbursing office. They are appointed to the position of paying agent as a
collateral duty and will be under the exclusive supervision of the disbursing agent in all matters concerning
custody and disposition of funds advanced to them. Funds advanced to a paying agent are held at personal
risk by the agent. Immediately upon completion of the transactions for which funds were advanced, the
paying agent must clear accountability with the disbursing agent. Paying agents provide the commander with
the capability to procure local goods and services. Disbursing agents must provide paying agents with
detailed instructions on finance policies regarding the expenditure of cash.
3-47. To execute local procurement support, the commander appoints a paying agent on an additional duty
appointment order. This appointment authorizes the paying agent to disburse public currency in accordance
with the special instructions stated in the appointment and the written instructions provided by the FIBN
commander. The field ordering officer (FOO) whom the paying agent supports receives separate instructions
from contracting officials. FOOs (appointed by the contracting officer) and paying agents train and work as
a team; the paying agent should participate with their FOO in training provided by the contracting officer.
Refer to DOD 7000.14-R Volume 5 and ATP 1-06.1.
3-48. The paying agent or FOO is held personally liable for any payment not in accordance with the
appointment orders or prescribed instructions. The paying agent cannot simultaneously serve as either a
certifying officer or FOO. The paying agent uses an official credit/debit card to make payments whenever
possible.

### METHODOFAPPOINTMENT OF APAYINGAGENT

3-49. Paying agents are appointed by their unit commander and approved by the FIBN commander in
accordance with DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 and the applicable theater policy. The appointment order
specifies the paying agent’s unit, the types of payments to be made, the duration of appointment, and the
maximum amount to be advanced.
3-50. The paying agent checks the appointment order for correctness and maintains a copy in possession
while performing duties. The paying agent forwards a copy of the appointment order to the disbursing officer
or designee to which the paying agent is appointed. The disbursing officer or designee keeps a copy of the
appointment order until the order expires or is revoked.
3-51. Newly appointed paying agents will immediately contact their disbursing officer or designee to receive
a detailed briefing before they are advanced currency. Paying agents receive from the disbursing officer or
designee a copy of the current paying agent instructions and must become thoroughly familiar with all
responsibilities. Paying agents sign a written statement acknowledging understanding and receipt of the oral
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**47**
**Chapter 3**
and written instructions. The statement will be permanently filed with the disbursing officer or designee. For
more information related to paying agents please refer to DOD 7000.14-R Volume 5 and ATP 1-06.1.

### APPOINTINGOFFICIAL'SRESPONSIBILITIES

3-52. The appointing official ensures that every paying agent entrusted with public funds is supplied with a
vault, safe, or other adequate secure container for exclusive use that is accessible only by that individual.
Never merge public funds with any other funds. Refer to DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 for storage container
specifications. Additional appointing official responsibilities include—
•
Developing and publishing a security program in accordance with command direction. The program
must provide instructions on adequate protection for funds and the maximum amount of public funds
and related documents and instruments that must be on hand at any given time.
•
Ensuring protection of funds is included in the overall disbursing security program. This includes
requirements for periodic review of the adequacy of the security measures being used and for testing
security equipment for proper operation on a semiannual basis.
•
Ensuring (when applicable) armed guards are made available to escort government funds to and
from the disbursing office when funds are being transported. The appointing official makes this
determination based on a risk assessment of the OE.
•
Providing fire protection for government facilities and funds.

### SAFEGUARDINGPUBLICFUNDS

3-53. Paying agents must acknowledge the receipt of funds by signing DD Form 1081 (Statement of Agent
Officer's Account) (advance funds). Paying agents are personally responsible for safeguarding these funds
until they are returned to the finance unit. Failure to safeguard funds may result in agents being held
personally liable for any funds that were lost. A paying agent may not entrust funds or accountable
instruments to any other person for any reason. All funds must be safeguarded in accordance with DOD
7000.14-R Volume 5.

### CLEARINGPAYINGAGENTS

3-54. Paying agents will make every effort to complete all payments and return funds and payment
documents as quickly as possible. The agent should return the funds and receipts as soon as tactically feasible
after providing the specified support, but no later than 30 days after the advance or as the disbursing officer
directs. All agents must clear their accounts with the funding disbursing officer or disbursing agent prior to
departing the theater of operations, regardless of circumstances. Upon final clearance of funds, both the
funding disbursing officer or disbursing agent and paying agent will sign a DD Form 1081 (clear funds). It
is recommended that the paying agent maintain a copy of the signed DD Form 1081.

### PAYINGAGENTCHALLENGES

3-55. Proper training of paying agents and separation of duties between paying agent and FOO are crucial to
deterring and preventing fraud, waste, or abuse of funds. Whenever possible, the paying agents and FOOs
should receive combined briefings and training on their respective duties and responsibilities. The following
list includes just a few of the challenges paying agents may encounter:
•
Corruption.
•
Customs and culture differences.
•
Trafficking in persons.
•
Enemy threats against vendors.
•
Information security and operations security (vendors can provide intelligence to the enemy).
•
Language barriers.
•
Time-management challenges.
•
Unauthorized commitments by the FOO or by someone representing the FOO.
•
Chain of command and conflicting responsibilities.
•
Contingency/combat environment.
**48**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Finance Operations**

### PAYINGAGENTSPECUNIARYLIABILITY

3-56. Pecuniary liability holds a person financially responsible for any illegal, improper, or incorrect
certification or payment. This is critical and has serious implications for individuals if not properly executed.
The U.S. Code and DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5 outline positions that assume pecuniary liability and the
processes to seek relief from pecuniary liability. Failure to maintain accountability of negotiable instruments
or improperly certifying documents for payment requires an investigation and diverts manpower from
operations to conduct the investigation.

## LOSS OF FUNDS

3-57. Irregularities are categorized as physical losses of funds; illegal, incorrect, or improper payments; or
overages of funds. Physical losses of funds are segregated further by major losses and minor losses. A major
loss of funds is a loss of $750 or more; any loss of funds resulting from theft, regardless of dollar amount; or
any loss, regardless of dollar amount, where there is evidence of fraud within the disbursing office. A minor
physical loss of funds is a loss of less than $750 without any evidence of fraud internal to the disbursing
office (DOD 7000.14-R Volume 5). All agents entrusted with funds have pecuniary liability. Also, all persons
not formally appointed as an agent but having government funds in their possession or control become
accountable for those funds as a part of their assigned duties, and they are subject to the same pecuniary
liability for the funds as if they had been formally appointed.

### SECTION III – BANKING

3-58. Banking support is an integral function in finance operations. The implementation of ecommerce into
contracting enables commanders to acquire mission-essential requirements during operations and bring
stability to a HN by strengthening that nation's banking system while legitimizing the government and
increasing the confidence in the HN's economy.

## OVERVIEW OF BANKING AND HOST-NATION SUPPORT

3-59. Banking support includes limited depositary selection and LDA establishment. USAFMCOM supports
banking initiatives across theater as it integrates, synchronizes, and coordinates with the HN as well as with
agencies including DOS, U.S. Treasury, DFAS, and Federal Reserve Banks. Banking support major tasks
include—
•
Identifying partner agencies and documenting economic analysis to support host countries.
•
Establishing theater Army banking policies and procedures.
•
Coordinating for ecommerce and supporting technology.
•
Coordinating the selection of any LDA with institutions in theater.
•
Coordinating for HN banking capability.

## HOST-NATION BANKING COORDINATION

3-60. During setting the theater and theater opening, the AFMC team, working in consultation with the TSC
G-8, will develop an assessment of the theater and HN banking requirements. The AFMC assessment will
consider infrastructure requirements necessary to increase HN financial systems stability, including central
and wholesale banking processes and procedures, and corresponding banking relationships. The HN's
banking laws, the Status of Forces Agreement, and other HN agreements require review to determine what
the banking rules are for foreign customers and which banking entities are permitted to operate within the
country.
3-61. The U.S. Embassy of the HN (a subset of the DOS) may be able to provide significant support to FM
operations through its economy officer, finance officer, and U.S. Treasury attaché. U.S. embassies maintain
an LDA with HN banks to facilitate procurement of funds. USAFMCOM (in conjunction with DOS and the
U.S. Treasury) provides the FISC information necessary to analyze the HN banking sector to open an LDA.
3-62. Until the establishment of an LDA, the AFMC/FISC can procure funds from U.S. embassies through
an already established LDA. Additionally, USAFMCOM (in coordination with the DOS and the U.S.
Treasury) may be able to provide detailed information about a country's banking sector, which will assist the
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**49**
**Chapter 3**
AFMC/FISC in the development of a banking support plan/initiative early in the operation. Where there is
no established banking infrastructure or system, the FISC procures funds in coordination with USAFMCOM
until the theater matures and an LDA is established. A screening on DOS and U.S. Treasury current list of
available banks should take place to expedite the selection process of an adequate bank or financial
institution.

### LIMITEDDEPOSITARYACCOUNT

3-63. A**limited depositary account****is a checking account in a United States or foreign commercial bank**
**that is designated by the Treasury Department to receive deposits from disbursing officers for credits**
**to their official limited depositary checking accounts**. Disbursing officers are responsible for the
maintenance and management of LDAs. They will ensure the amount of foreign currency purchased with
U.S. dollars, together with the balance on hand, is commensurate with immediate disbursing requirements
based on mission variables. Balances in excess of immediate needs (typically a five to seven business days'
supply) are promptly withdrawn and deposited with the Treasury or transferred to other DOD disbursing
officers, DOS disbursing officers, or a U.S. Embassy (if funding requirement is needed). This requirement
minimizes local currency operating bank balances and losses due to rate devaluations and avoids premature
drawdown on the U.S. Treasury's General Account. The disbursing officer maintains a record of every LDA
transaction to include payee names, voucher numbers, check amounts (in the original currency of the check),
check serial numbers, check dates, and the activity name under which the account is established. Periodic
statements from the depositary are used to reconcile the disbursing records. See DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 5
for more information on LDAs.
3-64. Disbursing officers review the LDA on a continuing basis to ensure that—
•
Maintenance of account balances are at the minimum necessary to meet immediate disbursement
needs (checks issued and in process) commensurate with the activity in the LDA.
•
Balances in excess of immediate needs (typically a five to seven business days' supply) are promptly
withdrawn and deposited with the Treasury.
•
The terms negotiated with the depositary are favorable to the government, including interest on the
account at the highest possible rate.

### CENTRALFUNDINGOPERATIONS

3-65. Central funding consolidates, acquires, distributes, and receives U.S. and foreign currencies to and from
disbursing officers and military banking facilities. Central funding provides the movement of cash
commodity in support of operations from the theater strategic to tactical level. Maintaining central funding
locations closer to units requiring currency minimizes risk to friendly forces, reduces the possibility of losing
cash commodities during transport, and frees transportation equipment for other sustainment requirements.
3-66. If the Army is appointed as theater executive agent for FM, the FISC serves as the theater central
funding proponent for all U.S. Services in theater. Restrictions may be imposed to prevent disruption of the
local economy and to prevent and control black market operations and counterfeiting.
3-67. As the theater matures and an FISC deploys, the FIBN transfers its central funding responsibilities to
the FISC. The FIBN provides the FISC with estimates of currency requirements weekly.
3-68. Finance units serve as the central funding elements, providing cash management/currency support for
the theater of operations. The disbursing officer is the central funding account holder and coordinates the
establishment of any LDA in theater.
3-69. Deployed FIBNs and other Service finance organizations will draw and return cash through the FISC.
In contingency operations where there is no FISC deployed, FIBNs must be prepared to resource cash
requirements in support of theater operations. In this instance, pre-deployment coordination with national
providers, agreements with approved funding sources, and establishments of LDAs are critical in support of
funding requirements.

### CURRENCYSUPPORTOPERATIONS

3-70. Currency support is the act of supplying foreign currencies, U.S. Treasury and local currency checks,
available ecommerce and, in some operations, precious metals to U.S. and multinational forces in the
**50**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Finance Operations**
operational area. Additionally, finance units provide currency and coins to Army and Air Force Exchange
Service facilities; tactical field exchanges; postal units; and companies serving as in-theater defense
contractors. The following are performed under cash management/currency support:
•
Perform cash acquisition and cost analysis.
•
Perform cash holding analysis authority (U.S. and foreign currency).
•
Document the applicable foreign currency market rates (for example, spot rate).
•
Determine the process and cost associated with acquisition and delivery of U.S. and local currency.
•
Obtain the budget rate for contracts paid in local currency.
•
Determine the availability of a foreign currency fluctuation account for local currency payments.
•
Determine the history, evaluation, and inflation of U.S. currency in relation to the local currency.
•
Determine which mechanism can acquire foreign currency at a better rate—ITS.gov or an LDA.
•
Acquire and account for currency (U.S. and foreign)/checks.
•
Dispose and report mutilated or damaged currency/checks.
•
Maintain and control agreements that support multinational forces, contractors, and DA Civilians.

### ECONOMICIMPACT OFUNITEDSTATESCURRENCY

3-71. Finance organizations analyze the economic impact of the use of currency (U.S. and foreign) on the
local economy. The analysis includes, but is not limited to, the cost of acquiring foreign currency, using
ITS.gov or an LDA, the availability of banking services and banking infrastructure, acceptance of local
currency by vendors, the impact of U.S. currency on the local economy, and review of Treasury and other
USG agency reports on the local economy. Close coordination with civil affairs, U.S. Embassy, U.S.
Treasury, and DOS officials will ensure accurate assessments.

### REDUCTION OFCASH INTHEATER

3-72. A stable and growing economy requires stabilization of inflation and purchasing power. Conflict, large
budgetary deficits, lack of public confidence in the government, the local currency, and other factors may
add to inflation instability and may cause a HN's residents to resist the national currency and convert their
funds to U.S. dollars, euros, or other foreign currencies. The mere presence of U.S. forces and unified action
partners will also directly and immediately affect the local economy. Without careful planning, U.S. forces
and unified action partner demand on local resources may create inflationary pressure. To minimize the
potential for inflationary pressure, finance should make all payments for goods and services in the local
currency, pay wages for local services at an appropriate wage-rate, and determine appropriate pricing levels.
Using the local currency helps put cash in the hands of the local populace, stimulates the economy, and
demonstrates confidence in the HN's government.

## MILITARY BANKING PROGRAM

3-73. Theater banking support will leverage existing programs and technology to identify and implement
new technologies and other resources to reduce U.S. dollars in theater. Leveraging technology improves the
FIBN's disbursing mission, improves the local banking system, and strengthens the economy across the
theater of operations to create an overall exit strategy. The banking team supports the FISC and USAFMCOM
in forging a strong partnership between the U.S. Army, U.S. Treasury, the DOS, and the Federal Reserve
Bank network. This effort should promote the identification and mitigation of capability gaps in finance
support of DOS economic goals within the theater pertaining to banking and ecommerce.
3-74. Military banking facilities’ main function during contingency operations is to provide cash
management/currency support. For more information about banking officer duties and responsibilities, refer
to ATP 1-06.3.

## BANKING SECTION

3-75. This section is responsible for theater currency (U.S. and foreign) support. The section coordinates with
HN support and military banking operations to establish and sustain the flow of currency to the FIBNs, other
DOD agencies and Services, and multinational forces in accordance with inter-service and intergovernmental
agreements; it may be designated to provide currency to all components of a joint or multinational force. This
section also coordinates with the State Department, local U.S. Embassy, USAFMCOM, DFAS, provisional
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**51**
**Chapter 3**
reconstruction teams, and/or the Treasury to establish HN banking support requirements. The banking section
helps coordinate the selection of any LDA institutions in theater and also facilitates the analysis of HN
financial institutions for LDAs. Additionally, it serves as the EFT assistance center for the AOR and as the
contracting officer representative for the U.S. Army Banking and Assistance Center (when in operation). The
banking section is responsible for oversight of theater ecommerce initiatives and programs as well as their
trends and analysis.

### SECTION IV - PAYMENT SUPPORT

3-76. Finance units provide a wide variety of payment support functions throughout their areas of operations.
Leaders at all echelons structure their sections to maintain separation of duties and proper internal controls
when conducting payment support operations to minimize potential fraud and abuse.

## OVERVIEW OF PAYMENT SUPPORT OPERATIONS

3-77. Payment support is the calculation of entitlements, verification and validation of appropriate
documentation, and certification of payments. Payment support includes the provision of full U.S. pay
support (including civilian pay not supported by DFAS), travel pay support, local and partial payments,
civilians, U.S. contractors, and non-U.S. pay support (for example, HN employees, day laborers, enemy
prisoners of war [EPWs], and civilian internees). Payment support operations also provide support to
noncombatant evacuation operations in the form of travel advances. Finance units must ensure that all
Soldiers, regardless of component, receive timely and accurate pay in accordance with existing statutes and
regulations.

## COMMERCIAL VENDOR PAY

3-78. Commercial vendor payment (CVP) support is for the immediate needs of the force. These are needs
that the standard logistics systems cannot support. FSTs and paying agents should try to use available EFT
payment methods or pay cash in the local currency if possible. Cash payments are usually for such items as
pay for day laborers, class I supplements (not otherwise on contract), and the purchase of construction
materials not readily available through the contract or supply system.
3-79. The commercial vendor services section certifies contract payments, prepares payment vouchers, and
forwards prepared vouchers to the disbursing section for payment. It monitors reports to reconcile contract
payments made, tracks invoices pending payment, and reconciles in-transit certified payments with post-
payment data received from disbursing. The commercial vendor services section manages payment
processing and works closely with the contracting battalion and its supporting G-8 to coordinate contracting
support and to ensure legality, prompt payment, and reconciliation of contract payments to identify unused
funds that may be redirected for other critical requirements.
3-80. Depending on mission variables and the maturity of theater, certain portions of transactional roles for
contract payments can be accomplished under the Home Station Mission Command Concept. It is possible
to retrograde CVP payments to finance organizations located outside of the AOR as a response to
unpredictable changes affecting normal operations in theater. Using CVP retrograde outside of the AOR as
an option to migrate workflow of contract payments can be accomplished with a "storefront" location within
theater, usually in consolidated support areas. This finance "storefront" receives and validates contract
payment documentation from requiring activities and contracting and retrogrades transactions, payment files,
and documents to out-of-theater units for payment processing and EFT disbursement. The FIBNs are
responsible for the technical oversight and assistance of the CVP retrograde mission.
3-81. This type of support increases during operations in lesser developed areas and remote sites. Local
procurement could help augment supply and service areas based on availability in theater:
•
Personal or non-personal service contracts for the performance of identifiable tasks rather than
furnishing an end item or supply.
•
Deliverables contracts for the furnishing of specific supplies.
•
Indefinite delivery/task order and blanket purchase agreement contracts awarded under simplified
acquisition procedures such as constructions contracts, cost contracts, lease contracts.
**52**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Finance Operations**

## SPECIAL AUTHORITIES PAY

3-82. Finance organizations support several special authority payment programs and carefully follow the
principles and standards prescribed by the Comptroller General of the United States. Some special payments
are classified based on their characteristics and the nature of the transactions they support. Solatium payments
are an example of special authorities pay.
3-83. A solatium payment is monetary compensation given to indigenous personnel to provide relief from
grief, suffering, and anxiety resulting from injury, property, or personal loss. Occasionally, solatium
payments are made to meet cultural expectations. A solatium payment is nominal in amount, and is not an
admission of liability by the U.S. The commander in whose area of operations the incident occurred is
responsible for determining entitlement to solatium payments. Finance units are responsible for the
disbursing and accounting of all solatium payments. AR 27-20 contains more information on solatium
payments.

## NON-U.S. PAY

3-84. Non-U.S. pay includes, but is not limited to HN employees, day laborers, EPWs, and civilian internees.
Finance must follow all prescribed laws, regulations, and theater policies while providing non-U.S. pay
support. Title 22, USC, Section 3968 establishes the pay for foreign national employees. Delegation of
authority is established by DODI 1400.25, Volume 1231. Authority is delegated to each military department
to re-delegate to its Service component commanders the authority to establish salaries, wages, fringe benefits,
related compensation items, and other terms of employment for foreign national employees. Finance units
are responsible for processing deposits to and payments from deposit, trust, or other special fund accounts.
They are also responsible for ensuring disbursements from these accounts are not made without specific
authority.
3-85. The AFMC or FISC develops policies and advises EPW camp commanders on EPW and civilian
internee operations that relate to pay and accounting. Upon request, units provide training to personnel
assigned to the camp or EPW unit. Additional guidance can be found in DODM 1416.08, which prescribes
procedures and instructions for the development of compensation programs for foreign nationals employed
by the U.S. Forces in foreign areas.

## MILITARY PAY

3-86. Until the full implementation of the Integrated Personnel and Pay System-Army (IPPS-A) and transfer
of military pay support to the human resources (HR) community, finance organizations will continue to
provide military pay support to all personnel in theater. Finance units ensure that personnel, regardless of
component, within the area of operations receive accurate and timely pay support. The impact of quality pay
support on Soldier morale cannot be understated. Thereby, military pay support remains a vital part of finance
operations.

### INTEGRATEDPERSONNEL ANDPAYSYSTEM- ARMY AND THEMILITARYPAYTRANSITION

3-87. IPPS-A and the military pay transition from finance to HR will make Soldiers, leaders, commanders,
and HR personnel jobs easier. Soldiers will resolve military pay issues primarily with S-1s, no longer needing
to work with finance offices for most personnel-triggered military pay actions. Commanders will track real-
time pay information for their Soldiers. HR professionals will be proficient in military pay policy and
fundamentals, which will help them resolve military pay issues.
3-88. The challenge for the components will be the major culture shift for the HR and finance communities.
Across the Army, communication and cooperation between the two organizations will be essential to a
seamless transition of the military pay mission passing from finance to HR.
3-89. The HQDA G-1 Functional Management Division is the Army's focal point and collaborative hub for
this effort, and it is responsible for planning and coordinating the transition as well as preparing the HR
community for the assumption of military pay functions. The military pay transition division is leading the
effort by documenting requirements, gathering feedback from the finance community, and sharing lessons
across both finance and HR.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**53**
**Chapter 3**

### ACTIVEDUTYPAY

3-90. FIBNs provide Active Duty payment support to all personnel assigned or attached within the
operational area. This support ensures that those personnel receive proper military pay entitlements and
allowances. Finance units will provide continuous payment support in an effective and efficient manner,
regardless of the operational tempo. Preventing backlog or late pay transactions is key to providing timely
pay and maintaining the good morale of the troops.
3-91. Brigade and battalion S-1s are the central link between Soldiers and changes to their pay entitlements.
S-1s are responsible for resolving routine pay inquiries through the timely submission of documents to the
FICO and distribution of supporting documentation affecting Soldier pay. See FM 1-0 for additional
information. Figure 3-1 depicts the flow of documents for military pay changes.
**Figure 3-1. Military pay support changes document flow**
3-92. The AFMC/FISC provides finance policy and technical oversight to all units in theater. **Technical**
**oversight****encompasses the provision of recommendations and advice to theater commanders regarding**
**the employment, integration, direction, and control of financial management forces for the**
**accomplishment of assigned missions.** Responsibilities and procedures are prescribed in accordance with
current regulations and directives. Separation of duties and adherence to required internal controls are strictly
enforced to prevent fraud and collusion. The internal control section in the FISCs and FIBNs ensure that all
functional sections maintain proficiency.
3-93. FIBNs provide payment support through their FICOs to all units and Soldiers in their area of operations.
The FIBNs perform the following military pay support functions:
•
Theater in and out processing.
•
Start/stop/change deployment entitlements (normally automated at theater entry and exit location).

•
Entitlement verification and certification.
