## RESOURCE MANAGEMENT FUNDING PROCESS

4-9. PPBE serves as the Army's primary RM process to obtain funding. The DOD Financial Management
Regulation defines PPBE as a cyclic process containing four distinct, but interrelated phases: planning,
programing, budgeting, and execution. In addition to establishing the framework and process for decision-
making on future programs, the process permits prior decisions to be examined and analyzed from the
viewpoint of the current environment (threat, political, economic, technological, and resources) and for the
time period being addressed. The ultimate objective of PPBE is to provide operational commanders the best
mix of forces, equipment, and support attainable within fiscal constraints.
4-10. PPBE is one of three main decision-support systems used by DOD civilian and military leaders to
allocate resources and manage defense acquisitions. Figure 4-1 depicts an overview of the PPBE process.
The main goal of the process is to establish, justify, and acquire the fiscal resources needed to accomplish
the Army's assigned missions in executing the National Military Strategy.
**Figure 4-1. The PPBE process**
4-11. Strategic, operational, and tactical levels all play an important role in the PPBE process. The Army's
strategic level has a fundamental and essential role. It involves setting overarching goals and priorities that
align with the national defense strategy. This level is responsible for making high-level decisions about
resource allocation, balancing risks and opportunities, and guiding the development of future capabilities.
This level is pivotal in setting the direction for the entire PPBE process. It integrates and synchronizes efforts
across various sections of the Army, ensuring that all actions and decisions made align with the broader goals
of national security and defense.  This responsibility extends beyond merely allocating resources. The Army's
strategic level is deeply involved in making decisions about where to invest for the future, which capabilities
to develop, and how to mitigate potential risks. At the operational level, the PPBE process is a systematic
method used to decide how to identify priorities, manage operations, and allocate resources. At the tactical
level, the PPBE process serves as a bridge to communicate needs and requirements.  This information is used
in the planning and programming stages to help decide which program to fund and where to apply resources.
4-12. The PPBE process allows the DOD to allocate funding resources to stay within its annual budget while
following the Secretary of Defense's policy, strategy, and goals. It enables commanders and leaders to make
resource-informed decisions to achieve the most effective use of resources. Resource managers are active
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**67**
**Chapter 4**
participants in the PPBE process at the theater strategic, operational, and tactical levels. PPBE is currently
an annual process.

### PLANNING

4-13. The first step in the process of allocating resources for the DOD is planning. In this phase, the Under
Secretary of Defense for Policy assesses strategic guidance such as the President's National Security Strategy,
the Secretary of Defense's National Defense Strategy, and the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff's National
Military Strategy. This assessment helps to coordinate the Defense Planning Guidance, which details force
development priorities and informs the programming phase. The objective of planning is to size, structure,
man, equip, train, and sustain the Army to support the National Military Strategy using The Army Plan, Army
Strategic Planning Guidance, and the Army Planning Priorities Guidance. In addition to Army guidance and
priorities, the joint staff and CCDRs impact on Army resources through their requirements submitted through
the Joint Strategic Review, Chairman's Program Assessment, and Combatant Commanders Integrated
Priority List.
4-14. The PPBE planning phase is a function that is designed to provide a vision of the future through broad
strategy and plans. This planning phase includes long-range guidance for up to 20 years, long-range
objectives for up to 10 years, and mid-range objectives for up to 5 years. Since the primary objective of the
planning process is to identify necessary requirements versus what is affordable, fiscal constraints must be
considered. However, in recent years, planning has become more fiscally informed.
4-15. There are several primary tasks that need to be accomplished during the planning phase. These include
gathering intelligence about the military capabilities and political intentions of foreign nations, assessing the
threat to U.S. national security, creating strategies to counter the threat, and determining the necessary force
levels to support the strategy.
4-16. Long-range plans are created to reflect the significant force modernization and investment requirements
for each DOD component. The planning process is influenced by the national priorities outlined in the
National Security Strategy and the activities of Congress.
4-17. In recent years, DOD has moved to a more centrally managed strategic planning process, particularly
since 2010. This change was prompted by the combination of changing operational requirements in Iraq and
Afghanistan and financial constraints that created a need for significant re-evaluation of U.S. strategy and
global objectives.
4-18. Within the Army, planning creates a vision of the Army 10 years into the future and beyond. Macro
estimates yield a specified size, composition, and quality of divisional and support forces. Derived from joint
strategic planning and intermediate objectives to achieve stated goals, this divisional and support force
provides the planning foundation for program requirements.
4-19. Planning sustains the Army's force structure by determining requirements and establishing
authorizations that support strategy and guidance. Total Army Analysis identifies the Army's force structure
requirements and then determines the means for resourcing those requirements to provide commanders with
the optimal mix of forces, equipment, and support to enable mission accomplishment.
4-20. At the strategic level, the Army sets its goals, objectives, and strategies during the planning phase. This
involves identifying the Army's requirements, capabilities, and potential threats. The strategic level also
determines the direction of the Army and establishes its priorities. At the operational level, units identify
their operational needs and requirements. These requirements may include various resources such as
personnel, equipment, and training, and are outlined in detail. The information provided by units significantly
shapes the Army's strategic goals and objectives and is crucial to inform decisions about resource allocation
and priorities. Finally, at the tactical level, units provide critical data to their higher echelon regarding their
operational needs, readiness status, and plans. This information may include details about personnel,
equipment, training, and sustainment requirements.

### PROGRAMMING

4-21. The programming process applies fiscal constraints to the OSD vision that was developed in the
planning phase. It is a military Service function that transforms guidance into actionable resourcing steps.
Programming involves allocating resources to plans, organizing plans into programs, prioritizing programs,
**68**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
and determining program affordability. The programming phase builds the POM consistent with the Army's
planning and OSD guidance. The objective of programming is to translate planning decisions and strategic
guidance into a comprehensive allocation of forces, manpower, and funds among competing requirements.
Commands participate in the PPBE process by ensuring all mission and operating requirements are known
through commander's program assessments, command-requested changes, briefing to the Program Planning
Budget Committee, and budget data calls. Mission essential requirements for manpower, operation and
maintenance (O&M), housing, and construction are integrated into centrally managed programs. The POM
represents the Army's proposal for a balanced allocation of its resources within OSD fiscal and manpower
constraints.
4-22. In the programming phase, specific programs are developed to achieve set goals by outlining necessary
capabilities and resources. At the strategic level, resource managers execute the POM, review program budget
decisions, submit reclamas to the DOD, and implement the approved POM. At the operational level,
identified needs are further refined into more specific plans. In coordination with HQDA and DOD, resource
managers participate in POM development and the submission of Management Decision Evaluation
Packages (MDEPs) to ensure that developed programs properly align with the strategic goals set out in the
planning phase. Specific programs and projects are determined based on the needs and requirements of the
units, translating the strategic plan into tactical actions. Resource managers at the tactical level contribute to
POM development by integrating command requirements into MDEPs for submission to the next higher
headquarters and ultimately to the ASCC for input into the POM.

### BUDGETING

4-23. Budgeting is the third phase of the PPBE process. In this phase, near-term resource requirements are
formulated and controlled, with a focus on allocation and use based on the results of planning and
programming efforts. The budgeting phase results in the creation of the Budget Estimate Submission, which
translates the POM into an appropriation format for congressional action. During budgeting, new-term
resource requirements and monetary allocation are formulated and justified based on the results of planning
and programming efforts.
4-24. During the budgeting phase, HQDA makes critical adjustments based on congressional interest and
direction changes. OSD reviews and updates the Budget Estimate Submission and issues the Program Budget
Decision incorporating any changes. After final review and update, OSD issues the Program Decision
Memorandum and submits the Army's information to OMB as part of the defense budget.
4-25. At the theater strategic level, specific programs are allocated resources based on their funding needs
and alignment with the Army's strategic objectives. Resource managers coordinate the Budget Estimate
Submission in accordance with HQDA and DOD elements.
4-26. Approved programs are translated into financial terms at the operational level. The costs associated
with each program are determined, and a comprehensive budget is created that aligns with the strategic goals
outlined in the previous stages. At this level, resource managers must pay meticulous attention to detail, as it
is critical to ensure that all costs are accounted for and that the budget is realistic and achievable. Resource
managers issue command budget estimate (CBE)-specific guidance and receive and consolidate CBEs and
spend plans. At the tactical level, resource managers prepare the CBE. It is essential to obtain input from
tactical units to ensure that the budget accurately reflects the required resources.
**Note.** The Government Accountability Office Cost Estimating and Assessment Guide describes
four characteristics for reliable cost estimates. They must be comprehensive, well documented,
accurate, and credible.

### EXECUTION

4-27. The final phase of the PPBE process is budget execution. Execution is the process by which
appropriated funds are obligated and the performance of the planning, programming, and budget formulation
phases are measured and validated. The execution phase occurs once Congress enacts authorization and
appropriations acts. During execution, assumptions that were made about a program's performance and
capabilities can be tested.  Risks taken during any previous phase (such as altering funding levels) are often
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**69**
**Chapter 4**
exposed.  The value in tracking execution data is the guidance provided to adjust future plans, programs, and
budgets based on actual events.  Congress enacts the budget based on its own program plans, so there will be
differences between the original defense budget request and the funding received.
4-28. During the execution phase of PPBE, the Army evaluates program results; this occurs concurrently
with the program review (in which programs that best meet strategic goals are prioritized), and the budget
review (in which spending amounts are determined). The execution phase assesses a program's actual
performance in comparison with its planned performance. The Army's GFEBS system plays a crucial role in
providing accurate and timely data to Army commanders at all levels in this assessment. GFEBS collects and
organizes financial data in a way that enables clear visibility and understanding of the commitment,
obligation, and disbursement of Army funds. Through the system, military leaders and decision-makers can
access and analyze the information, allowing them to make informed decisions.  GFEBS helps in shaping the
POM by providing a comprehensive view of the Army's financial situation, including details about
expenditures, available funds, and financial forecasts.
4-29. The main objective of budget execution is to manage and account for funds in support of approved
programs. Budget execution applies funds appropriated by Congress to carry out authorized programs during
the year of execution. The Army continuously monitors how well Army commands and program managers
use allocated resources to carry out program objectives through mid-year reviews, Dormant Account Review-
Quarterly, and Year-End Closeout. Comptrollers at all levels of command commit and obligate funds to
support mission essential requirements while establishing control and determining funding solutions within
congressional constraints.
4-30. In the execution phase, the theater strategic-level resource managers oversee program implementation
and budget spending to ensure efficiency and effectiveness. At the operational level, resource managers
distribute annual funding guidance, participate in mid-year reviews, receive and distribute funds, monitor
CBE execution of major subordinate commands, conduct program budget advisory committees, perform
unfunded requirement and decrement processing, and publish year-end guidance. They are responsible for
overseeing the utilization of allocated resources as per the plan and ensuring that programs achieve their
intended goals. Additionally, they conduct regular monitoring and evaluation of program success and make
any necessary adjustments to improve efficiency and effectiveness of operations. Programs and projects are
implemented at the tactical level. Resource managers receive and distribute funds in accordance with the
CBE, execute CBE, conduct unit-level program budget advisory committee meetings, perform unfunded
requirement and decrement processing, perform obligation tracking and management, perform fund
certification and control, track credits and credit turn-ins, monitor non-stock funds orders and payables,
perform government travel card oversight, and conduct year-end close out procedures.

## BUDGET, REQUIREMENTS, AND PROGRAM BOARDS

4-31. There will be a three-star Budget, Requirements, and Program Board co-chaired by the Deputy Chief
of Staff G-3/5/7, Deputy Chief of Staff G-8, and the ASA(FM&C). The board includes other Army Staff and
Secretariat principals as required when addressing issues in which those principals have an interest. This
board serves in an executive advisory role or resource-informed decision-making role to review significant
execution year or budget year resource allocation, requirements, or prioritization issues identified by senior
leaders, HQDA staff, the Army Requirements and Resourcing Board, the Army Campaign Plan forums, or
commands.
4-32. Budget, Requirements, and Program Boards receive support from the co-chairs' agencies. The three
planning program budget committee executive officers also support the boards by providing a central
clearinghouse and coordination function for board activities. The board publishes action and information
items, summaries of decisions, agendas, calendars, and meeting announcements in committee memorandums
or other documents.

### SECTION II - PROGRAMMING

4-33. The HQDA G-8 owns the POM for the Army. Programming involves the translation of decisions into
program and resource requirements, consideration of program alternatives, and the development of five-year
projections for forces, personnel, and funding. The main outputs of programming are the POM, the Budget
**70**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
Estimate Submission, Resource Management Decisions, and Future Years Defense Program (FYDP)
updates.

## PROGRAM OBJECTIVE MEMORANDUM

4-34. Every year during the programming phase, military departments and defense agencies are required to
submit a program request called the POM covering a five-FY period, and a budget request known as the
Budget Estimate Submission that addresses the first FY of the five-year POM submission. The POM and
Budget Estimate Submission serve as mechanisms for the Army to evaluate and right-size requirements and
programs. It covers the five-year FYDP and presents a proposal to the Army on how it can balance its resource
allocation. The POM is a recommendation from the Army to OSD regarding how the Army plans to allocate
funding for a program or programs to meet the Service priorities and the National Defense Strategy. It
includes an analysis of missions, objectives, and alternative methods to achieve those objectives. The Army’s
PPBE process operates on an annual cycle.
4-35. The POM prioritizes and adjusts programs in the FYDP. It describes the risks associated with not
funding or underfunding specific programs. Command operational requirements should be prioritized as
Mission Critical, Mission Essential, or Mission Enhancement and include urgent emerging requirements.
POM submissions and requests for funds should include tangible, defensible metrics, not unsubstantiated
generalities.

## PROGRAM REVIEWS

4-36. Program reviews are conducted to analyze the POM and help in translating guidance and objectives
into action. They assess how big the Army should be, what forces it should contain, what resources will be
needed, what should be bought, and where and what should be built. Additionally, fiscal constraints are taken
into consideration during program reviews.
4-37. Program reviews allow commanders to evaluate operational tasks accomplished during the POM. The
Army's role in the POM process is relatively straightforward. Every two years, the unit articulates what it can
do within the constraints of programmed funding or budget ceilings while identifying areas of need.

## RESOURCE MANAGEMENT DECISIONS

4-38. The OSD, Cost Assessment and Program Evaluation director conducts an annual review of the POM
developed by each DOD component. Throughout the year, OSD collaborates with DOD components to
modify programs via Resource Management Decisions. Resource Management Decisions are budget
decision documents that are released when analysts from OSD and OMB jointly review Service budget
submissions. They reflect Secretary of Defense decisions regarding the appropriate programs and funding to
be included in the annual defense budget request, which is part of the President's Budget.
4-39. Prior to submitting the POM, military Services complete a Budget Estimate Submission for the first
year of the FYDP. The USD(C) reviews the budget submissions under guidance from OMB. This review
ensures appropriate funding and fiscal controls, that the efforts are phased over the funding period, and that
execution is feasible within that budget year. At specific points throughout the year, particularly in the fall,
OSD collaborates with OMB to make budget changes via Revised Military Directives. Service Comptrollers
work with their service counterparts to review and ensure budget requests align with the unified defense
budget. Following the budget review, the Secretary of Defense may direct the Services to make changes.
Once any changes are made, the final budget will be submitted to OMB in December and included in the
President's annual budget request to Congress, which usually occurs in February.

## FUTURE YEARS DEFENSE PROGRAM

4-40. The FYDP is a comprehensive database that captures and summarizes information regarding all DOD
operations approved by the Secretary of Defense. The FYDP is divided into three primary dimensions: the
organizations affected such as military departments and defense agencies; appropriation accounts including
Research, Development, Test, and Evaluation and O&M; and Major Force Programs that comprise strategic
forces, mobility forces, and more.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**71**
**Chapter 4**
4-41. The 11 Major Force Programs include Strategic Forces; General Purpose Forces; Command, Control
Communications, Intel, and Space; Mobility Forces; Guard and Reserve Forces; Research and Development
Program; Central Supply and Maintenance Program; Training, Medical, and Other Personnel Activities
Program; Administration and Associated Activities Program; Support of Other Nations Program; and Special
Operations Forces.
4-42. The FYDP is a program and financial plan created by the DOD that is approved by the Secretary of
Defense. It outlines the distribution of programmed funds, manpower, and force structure over a period of
five years. This data is presented by Major Force Programs for internal review by the DOD to submit for
program and budget review. Moreover, it is also submitted to Congress in connection with the President's
Budget submission. The calendar year, budget year, and budget year +1 of the FYDP is organized into the
Congressional appropriation structure format and submitted with justification narratives to Office of the
USD(C) and OMB as the annual Budget Estimate Submission in September. Since the year of execution is
not complete, the calendar year is an estimate of execution. The budget year reflects the request from the
previous President's Budget. The budget year +1 is the current request undergoing OSD review and reflects
the proposed distribution of resources against programs and appropriations.
4-43.  Essentially, the FYDP acts as a crosswalk between DOD's internal accounting system and
Congressional appropriations through 11 Major Force Programs. The primary data element in the FYDP is
the Program Element. It is updated twice during the PPBE process cycle—once upon submission of the POM,
and secondly upon submission of the President's Budget.
4-44. The final phase of the FYDP budget cycle is the President's Budget. The President's Budget is submitted
by DOD to OMB in January and reflects OSD and OMB formal adjustments, known as decisions, to the
Budget Estimate Submission. It is the formal request for appropriations to Congress. The FYDP is an internal
working document of the DOD and is kept confidential within the DOD. As the programs beyond the budget
year are based on internal planning assumptions, the data is not shared outside the Executive Branch without
the explicit permission of the USD(C).

### SECTION III - BUDGET FORMULATION

4-45. The main objective of budget formulation is to allocate resources to mission priorities, define the
intended execution of those priorities, and request appropriations from Congress. **Budget formulation****is the**
**translation of programming decisions and commanders’ operational planning into an appropriation**
**format for justification to Congress**. During the budget formulation stage, the Executive Branch prepares
the President's Budget, and the Army Budget Office is responsible for budget formulation. The Army Budget
Office supervises and directs the development, submission, and defense of the President's Budget; transforms
the POM to the Budget Estimate Submission to the President's Budget; coordinates with the appropriation
committee, OSD, and OMB in accordance with laws and regulations; and implements guidance.
4-46. The budget formulation process is DOD's structured approach for preparing and submitting budgets to
Congress while aligning the correct appropriation to Army requirements per DOD's guidance. During budget
formulation, HQDA converts the first year of the POM into an appropriation structure to create the Budget
Estimate Submission. Meanwhile, budget justification materials are updated and developed to ensure that all
programs are reasonable and defensible to OSD. The budget formulation process enables commanders to
prioritize their requirements.

## STAGES OF BUDGET FORMULATION

4-47. The U.S. Army's budget formulation process is a comprehensive, multi-step procedure that involves
several key stages from preparation and justification to execution and audit of the Army's financial plan.
4-48. The process begins by determining the Army’s resource requirements for the upcoming FY. This stage
is crucial, as it involves identifying the Army's needs and calculating the cost. To assess the cost accurately,
various factors such as expected operations, maintenance, and personnel expenses are considered. It is a
comprehensive process that requires careful analysis and assessment to ensure accuracy.
**72**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**

### BUDGETREQUEST

4-49. Once the Army has determined the resources necessary for the upcoming year, it proceeds to prepare a
budget request. This document outlines the Army's financial requirements in significant detail, considering
all the different areas that require funding. The document provides a comprehensive breakdown of the costs
associated with various aspects of the Army's operations, including personnel, equipment, infrastructure, and
other critical areas. The budget request is a detailed document that outlines the total amount of funding
required and how it will be allocated to meet the Army's needs. It is a critical tool for ensuring that the Army
has the necessary resources to carry out its operations effectively and efficiently.
4-50. Once the budget request is prepared, it undergoes a detailed review and approval process facilitated by
the DOD and the OMB. During this stage, the budget request is carefully analyzed and scrutinized to ensure
that it meets the required financial and strategic objectives. The review process entails a detailed examination
of the budget request that focuses on the justifications provided and the feasibility of the proposed costs. The
DOD and OMB assess the budget request to ensure that it aligns with the government's overall financial plan
and national defense strategy. They also evaluate the budget request to determine if it is realistic and feasible
given the current economic climate.
4-51. This stage is crucial as it ensures that the budget request is aligned with the government's financial
goals and priorities. Additionally, it provides an opportunity to identify and address any issues or concerns
that may arise during the review process. By addressing these issues early on, the government can avoid any
potential setbacks or delays in the budget approval process. Overall, the review and approval process is a
critical step in the budgeting process, ensuring that the budget request is thoroughly analyzed and evaluated
before it is approved.

### BUDGETEVALUATION

4-52. Once the DOD and OMB have approved the budget proposal, it is then submitted to Congress. This is
where the budget is evaluated by various committees, each focusing on different aspects of the budget. These
committees analyze the budget in depth, examining details such as funding allocations, programmatic
changes, and policy implications. During the evaluation process, the committees may also ask for
justifications from federal agencies to explain certain budget requests. This ensures that the budget proposal
is transparent and that any concerns or questions are addressed. The committees may propose modifications
to the budget proposal. These modifications can take many forms, such as increasing or decreasing funding
for specific programs, adding or removing programs, or revising policy language. The proposed
modifications are then debated and voted on by the full Congress and, if passed, are included in the final
budget.

### EXECUTION

4-53. Once the budget is approved by Congress, it enters the execution phase, which is a critical stage in the
budget process. During this phase, the funds are allocated to Army units based on the approved budget. The
allocation process is crucial, as it determines how much each unit will receive to fund its operations. The
units within the Army that receive funding are required to utilize the funds in accordance with the budget
limits specified in the approved budget. This means that they must adhere strictly to the budget limits,
ensuring that they do not overspend or underspend. Overspending would result in a deficit, while
underspending would mean that the units did not utilize all the funds allocated to it. Units must also ensure
that they use the funds for the purposes for which they were allocated. This means that they cannot divert the
funds to other areas that are not covered in the approved budget.

### AUDIT

4-54. The final stage of the budget formulation process is a crucial step that involves an audit. The audit is a
comprehensive review of the use of funds to ensure that they have been utilized as intended and in accordance
with the budget plan. The purpose of the audit is to identify any discrepancies, irregularities, or instances of
misuse or misappropriation of funds. During the audit, financial records and transactions are thoroughly
examined to ensure that they align with the budget plan. The auditor closely scrutinizes the expenditures
made during the budget period and compares them with the budget plan. If there are any variances, they are
investigated to determine whether they are legitimate or not. Any discrepancies or irregularities found during
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**73**
**Chapter 4**
the audit are immediately addressed and corrective actions are taken to prevent such occurrences in the future.
The audit report is submitted to the concerned authorities for review and further action is taken if necessary.
The objective of the audit is to ensure transparency, accountability, and the effective utilization of funds
while preventing any misuse or misappropriation.

## APPROPRIATIONS AND AUTHORIZATIONS

4-55. The term “appropriations” refers to the decisions taken by Congress regarding the allocation of a part
of federal spending. It is important to note that no appropriation can be made without an authorization.
Authorization bills are responsible for creating, extending, or making changes to statutes and specific
programs. They also specify the amount that appropriators can spend on a particular program. Once the
authorization bills are passed, the appropriations bills provide the discretionary funding available to agencies
and programs that have already been authorized.
4-56. Congress exercises the power granted to it under the U.S. Constitution to appropriate funds. Congress
has further exercised this legislative power by setting legal limits on USG officials.
4-57. The President has an important role in the appropriations process by virtue of constitutional power to
approve or veto entire measures unless Congress overrides a veto. The President also has influence, in part,
because of various duties imposed by statute, such as submitting an annual budget to Congress.
4-58. As depicted in figure 4-2, the President initiates the appropriations process by submitting the President's
Budget for the upcoming FY to Congress. The President is required to submit the annual President's Budget
on or before the first Monday in February. The President recommends spending levels for various programs
and agencies of the federal government in the form of budget authority because Congress provides budget
authority to agencies as opposed to cash. When the President submits a budget to Congress, each agency,
such as the DOD, generally provides detailed justification materials to the House and Senate appropriations
subcommittees that have jurisdiction over its funding.
**Figure 4-2. Appropriation and authorization process**
4-59. Figure 4-2 begins its depiction of the appropriation/authorization process with both the House of
Representatives and Senate considering the appropriations measures simultaneously. Traditionally, the
Senate would wait until it received the House-passed bill to begin its consideration of the appropriation
measure. During the House's consideration of the bill, the House Appropriations Committee (the House
**74**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
Armed Service Committee for the authorization bill), through its defense and MILCON appropriation sub-
committees with jurisdiction over the defense and MILCON appropriation, amends the draft version of the
bill. Upon completion, it then reports the bill to the House floor for consideration and passage. After passage,
the House sends the bill to the Senate. The Senate Appropriations Committee (the Senate Armed Service
Committee) through its sub-committee with jurisdiction over the defense MILCON appropriation, amends
the House version of the bill and reports the bill to the Senate floor for additional amendments and
considerations. Both chambers have their own rules regarding amending appropriation bills. The Senate then
passes its version of the appropriations/authorization bill.
4-60. If the Senate version and House versions do not match, a conference committee is formed to
compromise and create a bill that will pass both houses of Congress. The updated conference committee
appropriations bill then goes back to the House and Senate for final approval. The approved version is then
sent to the President.
4-61. When the President receives the bill from Congress, the President has 10 days to sign or veto the bill.
If the President signs the bill, it immediately becomes law. However, if the President vetoes the bill, it will
be sent back to Congress. Congress may override the veto by a two-thirds vote in both houses. If Congress
successfully overrides the veto, the bill becomes law. If Congress is unsuccessful, the bill dies.
4-62. In instances when Congress and the President do not complete passage of the regular appropriations
acts prior to the beginning of the new FY, Congress will pass a continuing resolution to fund current
government operations beyond 1 October of the new FY. A continuing resolution is an appropriation act that
provides budget authority for federal agencies such as DOD to continue operation in the absence of a regular
appropriation act. Resource managers must refer to the continuing resolution to determine the exact authority
authorized for that particular time frame.
4-63. A funding gap results when neither an appropriation act nor a continuing resolution exists, and current
budget authority expires at the end of the FY. Operations during a funding gap are considerably limited and
operational effectiveness significantly hampered.
4-64. Requirements must be funded by the appropriation for the intended purpose of that requirement to
ensure funds are appropriated correctly, ultimately preventing violation in accordance with Section 1301 of
Title 31, USC, otherwise known as the “Purpose Statute”. Budgets are submitted for each appropriation to
enable Congress to see the amounts and justifications for each appropriation separately. DOD 7000.14-R,
Vol 2 provides general guidance on the formulation and submission of each appropriation budget request to
OSD and Congress. This volume is divided into two books because of the volume size. Volumes 2A and 2B
are both necessary for the formulation and preparation of the budget and are separated into chapters based on
appropriation and special topics.

### SECTION IV - BUDGET DISTRIBUTION

4-65. By law and regulation, each DOD component must design systems for administrative control of funds
so that formal administrative subdivisions of funds are placed at the highest practical organizational level
consistent with effective and efficient management. Additionally, each component shall restrict the use of
limitations on available funds to those necessary to comply with statutory provisions in keeping with the
appropriate DOD authorization or appropriation act.
4-66. Funds are distributed either formally (formal subdivision of funds) or informally (informal subdivision
of funds) from higher-level organizations to lower-level organizations within the executive branch and the
Office of the USD(C). Enterprise Funds Distribution and GFEBS are the official funds control management
systems for DOD. OSD’s ability to release funds depends on three steps after the appropriation becomes law:
•
Step 1—Apportionment from OMB.
•
Step 2—A warrant issued by the U.S. Treasury showing the amount of cash on the account or
appropriation.
•
Step 3—The issuance of a release letter by OSD, meaning the Defense Department now has the
money.
4-67. Allocations are formal distributions of apportionments or funds that do not require apportionment by
the departmental-level accounting entity to an operating agency or other intermediate-level accounting entity.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**75**
**Chapter 4**
An allocation of funds represents an authorization by a designated official of a DOD component making
funds available within a prescribed amount to an operating agency for the purpose of making allotments and
incurring obligations. Allocations carry the same legal and other limitations as apportionments. Allocations
of budget authority from the Service department to Army commands, ASCC direct reporting units, and
operating agencies must be in writing. Allocations, sub allocations, or their parts, that are not required to be
further subdivided may be treated and recorded as allotments. Using GFEBS, HQDA issues funding
authorization documents to allocate obligation authority and program authority to Army commands, ASCCs,
and operational areas.
4-68. An allotment is a formal distribution of budget authority to an execution-level accounting entity (for
example, from an Army command to a subordinate command). It authorizes the incurrence of obligations
within a specified amount. Sub allotments may be used to further subdivide the budget authority. Accounting
for a sub allotment is the same as accounting for an allotment; only the term allotment will be used in this
chapter. Army commands, ASCCs, and operating agencies may issue funding authorization documents to
their units to allot obligation authority and program authority.
4-69. Commands may informally subdivide funds at lower levels without creating an absolute limitation on
obligation authority. These subdivisions are considered funding targets. Targets, referred to as “allowances,”
represent informal subdivisions of funding. The lowest formal distribution of funds is at the ASCC level with
funding allowances being issued to subordinate units. This system allows more flexibility in fund control and
lessens the possibility of reportable Antideficiency Act violations.
4-70. Commanders are still responsible for executing their mission within the provided funding allowance,
and violations of that guidance may warrant administrative disciplinary action. Exceeding their funding
target/allowance does not constitute a statutory violation but could result in an over-obligation or over
expenditure of the Army command or ASCC funding authority. Regardless, persons responsible for
exceeding their target/allowance will be identified by name as being responsible for any resulting
Antideficiency Act violation.

### SECTION V - BUDGET EXECUTION

## FUNDING SUPPORT

4-71. The funding support mission is to analyze resource requirements, ensure commanders are aware of
existing resource implications in order for them to make resource-informed decisions, and then obtain the
necessary funding that allows the commander to accomplish the overall unit mission.
4-72. Resource managers identify various funding requirements and responsibilities during operations that
include offensive, defensive, and stability operations as well as defense support of civil authorities. G-8s may
have to support units conducting these operations simultaneously. They support the commander’s operation
by providing funding in compliance with established statutes and regulations. Resource managers have
various funding sources available and advise commanders on the use of those funds accordingly. Resource
managers should leverage ERP systems to automate and manipulate financial data in order provide costs,
project command spend plans, and submit future year budget estimates.
4-73. G-8s incorporate cost awareness across each phase of operations within operational limits. The more
mature a theater becomes, the more G-8s will have the ability to implement financial data analytics and
internal controls and improve the overall funding process.

### THEATERSTRATEGICFUNDING

4-74. At the theater strategic level, the ASA(FM&C) manages PPBE, which serves as the Army’s primary
RM process in obtaining funding.
4-75. Funding at the theater strategic level is engaged in estimating, budgeting, planning, costing, and
defending funding requirements for operations in support of national goals to senior Army leaders, the
Secretary of Defense, the President, House and Senate appropriation committees, and ultimately to Congress.
When in support of a declared named operation, funding is requested through the supplemental process.
Congress will expedite approving appropriations should it be necessary (for example, funding in support of

**Resource Management**
Hurricane Katrina). In all deployment operations, the Army will be required to report to OSD detailed cost
reports, by cost structure, and all the funding used in support of the deployment in accordance with DOD
7000.14-R Volume 12. Table 4-1 identifies specific tasks and sub-tasks that are associated with the strategic
fundamentals of funding support.

### OPERATIONALFUNDING

4-76. Funding at the operational level is engaged in resourcing requirements; identifying, acquiring, and
distributing funds; and tracking, analyzing, and reporting execution in support of stated objectives to senior
Army leaders and the Army budget office. The corps G-8 will coordinate with the FISC for FM support at
the operational level. The corps G-8 is also responsible for complying with ASA(FM&C) published guidance.
Special attention should be given to ensuring accurate and timely reporting in accordance with the published
guidance. Additionally, the corps G-8 is responsible for providing specific RM guidance, policy, and
instructions that influence the corps and below level commander requirements. Theater funding guidance is
published in the form of an appendix to the theater OPORD or fragmentary order and implemented by
operational level G-8s across the OE. The appendix gives detailed instructions G-8 staff need to follow to
meet requirements for funding. Theater funding guidance should include—
•
Overview of the types of funding and uses/purpose.
•
Evaluating ERP systems requirements necessary for funding tasks.
•
Requirements validation with resource boards (for example, Defense Acquisition Review Board),
the approval process, and thresholds.
•
Risk Management and Internal Control Program with stated objectives and frequency.

Budget execution and cost management guidance.
•
Special program funding describing proper uses and responsibilities.

### OPERATIONALNEEDSSTATEMENT

4-78. Operational commanders use the Operational Needs Statement (ONS) process to mitigate operational
capability gaps, to correct a high-risk deficiency, to accomplish the mission and reduce the risk of
catastrophic loss of life. The Army Requirements and Resourcing Board serves as a senior leadership forum
through which the Secretary of the Army and the Army Chief of Staff review, prioritize, and approve Army
policy and resource allocation issues.
4-79. The purpose of the ONS process is to adjudicate and reprioritize the allocation of critical Army assets
to include funding to meet operational needs. High risk deficiencies addressed in the ONS process are
primarily class VII, class VIII, and nonstandard equipment. Other classes of supply are addressed through
the logistics enterprise.
4-80. It is important for commanders to be aware of and understand how to use the ONS process. Resource
managers at each echelon can assist the commander with developing and submitting ONS. Detailed
information for ONS can be found in AR 71-9.
**78**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**

### TACTICALFUNDING

4-81. Funding at the tactical level is engaged in resourcing command requirements; identifying, acquiring,
and distributing funds; and tracking, analyzing, and reporting execution. Limited cost modeling can be
conducted at the tactical level, but normally will require augmentation from the theater strategic or
operational level.
4-82. The division G-8 will be responsible for complying with published RM guidance from higher
headquarters and is responsible for providing funding guidance and establishing management internal control
requirements. The division G-8 is responsible for briefing the division commander on theater-specific
funding available, making fiscal recommendations, and receiving approval of the division funding guidance.
Division funding guidance is published in the form of an appendix to the division OPORD. The appendix
gives detailed instructions that the division staff and subordinate brigades need to follow to meet requirements
for funding.
4-83. Comptrollers assigned or attached at all levels enable commanders to make resource-informed
decisions. Table 4-3 gives specific tasks and sub-tasks that are associated with the tactical fundamentals of
funding support.

4-84. Along with enforcing theater published guidance, the division G-8 is responsible for ensuring the most
effective and efficient use of resources. The division G-8 must ensure there is an effective requirements
approval process. The division G-8 collaborates in the staff process that aligns the requirement with the
functional staff proponent who can most effectively review, consider alternatives, and justify the requirement
to the approving authority. This process known as division requirements review board, which can be either
an electronic process or can be a standing meeting where board members attend on a recurring basis as
determined by workload.
4-85. The G-8 must work with the SJA. The legal review is an important component of any requirements
packet, and the SJA should be part of any requirements board in order to give the commander (or the chief
of staff) an appropriate review. Care should be given when dealing with complex funding issues and relying
on the fiscal lawyer’s opinion on the legality of the requirement and the proper use of funds. The SJA opinion
**21 April 2025**
