**79**
**Chapter 4**
does not grant relief from a potential anti deficiency act violation, as the fund certifier is solely responsible
for ensuring the appropriateness of funding.
4-86. The brigade S-8 uses published RM guidance, policies, and fiscal law to plan and execute RM
operations. The S-8, as a critical member of the brigade principal staff, ensures the commander’s operational
requirements are properly resourced. The S-8 is in constant synchronization with the fiscal triad and other
staff to project funding requirements and oversee tactical execution of spending. The S-8 also ensures that
the commander is aware of existing funding implications to make resource-informed decisions and that
requirements are properly validated. In addition to these functions, aviation brigade S-8s must understand
and be intricately involved in managing the command’s flying hour program in coordination with the division
G-8.

## FUNDING IN MULTINATIONAL ENVIRONMENT

4-87. Multinational operation is a collective term describing military actions conducted by forces of two or
more nations, typically organized within the structure of a coalition or alliance. An alliance is the relationship
that results from a formal agreement (for example, a treaty) between two or more nations for broad, long-
term objectives that further common interests of the members (for example, the North Atlantic Treaty
Organization [NATO]). A coalition is an ad hoc arrangement between two or more nations for common
action (for example, Operation Iraqi Freedom). FM considerations (including reimbursement policies and
procedures) for ad hoc arrangements are similar to those for support to United Nations (UN) operations as
prescribed in DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 12.
4-88. Non-traditional military operations such as peacekeeping operations and foreign humanitarian
assistance have evolved alongside the traditional forms of military action (deterrence and armed conflict). In
the future, international organizations and agencies will perform an increasing role in the management of
these crises and contingency operations. The level of U.S. participation in these operations depends on the
objectives agreed to at the national level. Nations must also reach agreement, separately or through
cooperative agreements, to provide resources or specific support requirements for their forces. FM
considerations depend on the nature of the supported multinational operation and vary from one operation to
another. These considerations may come from financial arrangements of support based on specific coalition
agreements, memoranda of understanding, or technical agreements. It is important to begin coordination of
financial arrangements with prospective multinational partners as early in the planning process as possible.
4-89. The following paragraphs describe UN, NATO, HN support, and multinational operations and other
operations with respect to FM support.

### UNITEDNATIONSOPERATIONS

4-90. U.S. forces may conduct a variety of missions in conjunction with the UN. Section 7 of the United
Nations Participation Act authorizes support to UN peacekeeping operations. This authority allows the DOD
to contribute personnel, nonlethal equipment, supplies, and services to UN operations. Support provided to
the UN under this act does not require the negotiation of an agreement. However, formal agreements, such
as Foreign Assistance Act (FAA) section 607 and section 628 agreements, and UN Letters of Assist (LOAs),
are established in most cases to facilitate reimbursement for services provided.
4-91. Any support provided to UN forces must be pre-approved by a UN official authorized to commit funds.
This official is normally the chief administrative officer or the chief procurement officer. Activities
undertaken, troops deployed, or costs incurred for items that are not agreed to in advance by the UN (as
identified and detailed in guidelines, aide-memoire, notes verbal, or specific or general LOAs) are normally
not reimbursed by the UN. Financial responsibilities of each party are usually spelled out in the agreement
between the contributing countries and the UN. The U.S. position is normally negotiated by the DOD in
coordination with the DOS. Close coordination with UN officials throughout the operation should ensure
proper reimbursement for all authorized expenditures.
4-92. Two types of documents are critical to FM when participating with the UN. The first are standing
agreements that are in place related to contributing country participation in a UN operation. The second are
LOAs.
**80**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
**Standing Agreements**
4-93. Standing agreements are general in nature and provide guidelines on what the UN is willing to pay for
without any additional, specifically negotiated agreements. An understanding of these documents early in an
operation is essential to ensure proper reimbursement for U.S. participation in a UN operation. The following
are some examples of the types of support standing agreements:
•
Pre-deployment actions. Preparation of personnel and equipment for deployment is the
responsibility of the contributing country and includes all preparation costs involved to get the
personnel or equipment to the point of embarkation. Billing the UN for reimbursement of these
expenses is based on advance negotiations with the UN.
•
Deployment and redeployment actions. Transportation to and from the operational area is normally
by airlift or sealift. The UN can fund all deployments and redeployments. The contributing
government may organize these activities, but the UN must agree to the arrangements in advance.
All transportation to be provided by the contributing country must be coordinated and approved by
the UN. If reimbursement is requested, it will be made only up to the amount it would have cost the
UN to accomplish the move.
•
Self-sufficiency period. Each contingent force must be self-sufficient in theater until UN operations
and control are sufficiently established to provide sustainment. The UN will reimburse normal and
agreed-to costs incurred during the self-sustainment period. All deployed military units should be
self-sufficient in rations, water, petroleum, oils, and lubricants for a minimum of 30 days, and other
classes of supplies for a minimum of the first 60 days after deployment.
**U.N. Letter of Assist**
4-94. An LOA is a document, issued by the UN to a contributing government, authorizing that government
to provide goods or services to the UN. An LOA details specifically what is to be provided by the contributing
government and establishes a funding limit that cannot be exceeded. General support LOAs can be negotiated
with the UN (if such LOAs are advantageous to both parties) to cover more generic categories such as
subsistence; petroleum, oils, and lubricants; sustainment; and repair parts. LOAs are considered by the UN
to be contracting documents and must be signed and issued by an authorized UN official.
4-95. The UN issues the approved LOA to the U.S. Mission to the UN, where it is acted on by the military
advisor. The Office of the Military Advisor determines the appropriate U.S. agency to receive the request.
All requests from the DOD are forwarded to the Under Secretary of Defense for Policy for approval and
action. The Under Secretary of Defense for Policy will determine the appropriate organization and provide a
copy of the LOA to the organization and DFAS. DFAS is responsible for maintaining the status of all active
LOAs.
4-96. The LOA is not considered a funded order, and the UN does not ordinarily provide an advance of funds
for the value of the request. Therefore, an LOA does not provide to a Service department any additional
obligation authority to accomplish the order. The Service Department must accomplish the requirement using
existing O&M funds or other appropriated funds and prepare an SF 1080 (Voucher for Transfers Between
Appropriations and/or Funds) bill for the cost of the goods or services provided, referencing the appropriate
LOA.

### NATO OPERATIONS

4-97. NATO is an alliance of independent member countries from Europe and North America. Under Article
5 of the NATO Treaty, an attack upon the territorial integrity of a treaty member is considered an attack on
all member states. While mutual defense remains the primary mission of the alliance, NATO has recently
expanded its sphere of activities to include peace support missions outside the territorial boundaries of the
member nations. Such non-Article 5, or “out-of-area,” operations may be undertaken at the request of the
UN with the unanimous consensus of all member states.
**NATO Funding Eligibility**
4-98. NATO conducts missions on the basis of force and capability contributions from its member states.
Unlike the UN, it does not provide reimbursement for peacekeeping forces or in any other way underwrite
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**81**
**Chapter 4**
the costs of national participation. As a result, NATO operating budgets are small in relation to those of
national forces deploying in support of an operation. NATO funding is generally restricted to establishment
and support of the NATO headquarters in theater. In exceptional circumstances, the NATO nations may fund
theater-wide projects benefiting both the NATO headquarters and all nations with forces in theater (for
example, theater communications systems or certain engineering projects supporting main supply routes or
ports). The NATO headquarters may establish a multinational logistics center to coordinate this effort among
the forces in theater.
**NATO Funding Sources**
4-99. The support of national forces in the theater is a national responsibility and is funded through national
systems and budgets. In the exceptional circumstance that a category of expenditure might be considered
eligible for NATO funding, the requirement must be submitted through the NATO theater headquarters for
inclusion in the budgetary plans described above. NATO funds its C2 structure through two primary sources,
which are discussed in the following paragraphs.
4-100. The NATO Security Investment Program is generally used to support major investments in
operational infrastructure, such as construction or communications systems. Projects originate with the
NATO theater headquarters engineers and are subsequently screened by the NATO chain of command prior
to being reviewed by the NATO Infrastructure Committee at NATO headquarters in Brussels, Belgium.
Funding is approved and provided on a project-by-project basis. Funds cannot be transferred between
projects.
4-101. The NATO military budget is the normal source of funding for O&M costs supporting NATO
headquarters in the theater of operations. NATO theater headquarters funding requirements are assembled
by the theater financial controller and consolidated into an operational budget. This budget is screened by the
NATO chain of command prior to submission to the Military Budget Committee, also located in Brussels.
Funding is approved in accordance with the proposed expenditure plan; however, there is generally some
flexibility between budgetary line items.
**Centralized Contracts**
4-102. To reduce competition for resources in the theater, the NATO headquarters may solicit and
consolidate theater-wide requirements to negotiate basic ordering agreements with local vendors. Such
agreements typically establish the prices, ordering procedures, and payment terms, but do not obligate NATO
or its member nations to purchase specific quantities of goods or services. While member nations are
generally authorized to utilize such contracts on the same basis as the NATO headquarters in-theater, the
existence of such agreements does not preclude nations from negotiating their own bilateral contracts. NATO
does not finance national requirements; such contracts call for nations to make direct payment to vendors for
goods and services they order.

## HOST-NATION SUPPORT REIMBURSEMENT PROCEDURES

4-103. Once the bilateral international agreement providing HN support is established, the S-4, G-4, or J-4
provides a detailed statement of requirements to the HN and obtains the agreed-upon logistics support.
Operations and Maintenance, Army (OMA) funds are normally used to support HN requirements. Specific
procedures for cost capturing and billing must be negotiated with the HN and the U.S. This will prevent
locally negotiated agreements that may not be legal or authorized. Finance organizations must submit the SF
1080 to DFAS with sufficient detailed documentation and a certified contingency operations cost report to
support the request for reimbursement.
4-104. Financial managers must advise commanders of the importance of dealing with foreign authorities
during the reimbursement of funds. Each instance must be reviewed carefully and discussed with legal
experts during the fiscal triad process to corroborate the authenticity and legality of the claims.

### CIRCUMSTANCES FORSUPPORT

4-105. Foreign nation support is civil and/or military assistance rendered to a nation when operating outside
its national boundaries during military operations based on agreements mutually concluded between nations
**82**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
or on behalf of intergovernmental organizations (JP 3-80). This support is generally provided under one of
three circumstances:
•
Support can be provided under the existing rules of a parent organization controlling the operation
(for example, NATO or the UN). Billing procedures under these circumstances should follow
standing agreements for support.
•
Support may be provided if the U.S. and the supported country have a bilateral agreement in place
prior to the operation. The U.S. has many of these cooperative agreements with allies. The financial
manager must consult with the legal advisor for a copy of any existing bilateral agreements and
follow the procedures outlined in the agreement for reimbursement.
•
Support can be provided based upon an agreement negotiated expressly for the operation. Any
negotiated agreement for support should include billing and reimbursement instructions.

### BILLING

4-106. Bills prepared for support during a UN or NATO operation should follow procedures established by
those organizations. Bills prepared for either standing or negotiated bilateral support agreements should be
processed as set out in the agreement. DOD strengthens key international partnerships through the acquisition
and transfer of logistics support, supplies, and services between DOD and certain countries and regional
organizations. These acquisitions and transfers are accomplished on a reimbursement basis, or by
replacement-in-kind, or by exchange of supplies or services of an equal value exchange.

### ASSISTANCE INKIND

4-107. Assistance in kind operations, which deal directly with the governments of eligible countries,
exchange material and services with foreign nations for materials and services of equal value. These items
are accountable as future reimbursements to the country that initially provides them on a gratis basis. Costs
for these items have a current value that is captured as future reimbursements. Resource managers at the JTF
develop and implement procedures in coordination with sustainment elements to track the value of support
provided to ensure an equal exchange of valued materials and services throughout the operation. Particular
care must be taken in accounting for these authorized exchanges due to the political sensitivity inherent in
multinational operations. Ideally, these in-kind reimbursements should derive no monetary gain and should
provide mutual benefit and equity between the participating countries.

## NONGOVERNMENTAL ORGANIZATION REIMBURSEMENT

## PROCEDURES

4-108. A nongovernmental organization is a private, self-governing, not-for-profit organization dedicated to
alleviating human suffering; and/or promoting education, health care, economic development, environmental
protection, human rights, and conflict resolution; and/or encouraging the establishment of democratic
institutions and civil society (JP 3-08). NGOs do not operate within the military or governmental hierarchy.
However, because NGOs operate in remote areas of high risk, they may need the logistics, communication,
and security support that military forces can provide. Expectations of military support (including supplies,
services, and assistance) must be reviewed with the organizations. Financial managers must consult with a
legal advisor to determine the authority to provide support on a reimbursable or non-reimbursable basis. Each
organization normally has some type of financial control officer. Military activities should provide support
only after they receive approval. Financial managers should ensure all supply activities, especially fuel,
maintain a status of what is provided. Supported organizations are billed as required, and if the organization
is not authorized to make payment locally, it should forward the documentation (signed by both
organizations) through Service funding channels.

## NON-DEPARTMENT OF DEFENSE GOVERNMENTAL AGENCIES

## REIMBURSEMENT PROCEDURES

4-109. Congress provides the DOD with funds for very specific needs. Therefore, support directly to other
USG agencies can be complex. When presented with a request for support by a USG agency, financial
managers should consult with their legal advisor. A memorandum of agreement or letter requesting support
should form the basis for any reimbursable relationship with non-DOD governmental agencies. This
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**83**
**Chapter 4**
agreement can be used to ensure only authorized support is provided, and that supply and service activities
capture the cost of support. Bills should be compiled as required, using a manual SF 1080 through the
supported agency. The SF 1080 must have a copy of the agreement with attached substantiating documents.

### SECTION VI - ACCOUNTING

4-110. Accounting support requires the close participation of ASCC, corps, and division G-8s and FISC,
FIBN, and FICO personnel supporting DFAS activities in ensuring the proper recording, analyzing,
verifying, and reporting of financial data associated with Army activities. Most important, effective financial
data capturing is achieved through a joint effort between the FM community, contracting personnel, and the
use of applicable FMIS. There are specific types of accounting pertinent to RM such as budgetary accounting,
proprietary accounting, and managerial cost accounting.
4-111. A commander must know the status of funds (funding balance, state of financial execution) to make
informed resourcing and operational decisions. Resource managers obtain this information for the
commander by tracking commitments, obligations, accrued expenditures/expenses, and disbursements daily.

## STAGES OF A TRANSACTION

4-112. Transactions are processed through stages that culminate with the recording of a disbursement or
payment for goods or services. Figure 4-3 depicts the stages of a transaction.
**Figure 4-3. Stages of transaction**

### AUTHORIZATIONSTAGE

4-113. The accounting process begins with an authorization. Resource managers receive their Fund
Authorization Document, DD Form 448 (Military Interdepartmental Purchase Request), Resource
Allocation Documents, and Resource Distribution Documents via the Program Budget Accounting System
or GFEBS. The supported unit will request funds for services or goods; however, only resource managers
(G-8/S-8) validate the availability of those funds through funds certification.

### COMMITMENTSTAGE

4-114. A commitment is an administrative reservation of funds where the funds certifying official certifies
that funds are available for an approved requirement. The funds certifying official ensures funds are available
and sets them aside for the requirement. Varied accounting systems are used to track commitments. These
accounting systems track costs (by event, program, unit, MDEP, and Army Management Structure Code for
each transaction) based on the fiscal code. The fiscal code helps track expenditures at a detailed level. It also
helps prepare and present fiscal information to the command and staff, including the status of funds, mission
or event cost, status of unfinanced requirements, and obligation rates.
4-115. A **cost center****is** **a base for a management optimization model utilized to reflect the business, its**
**inputs, conversions, and outputs in order to support management decisions.** A cost center is a locally
established data element that allows the ability to capture costs and track commitments, obligations, accrued
expenditures/expenses, and disbursements in accounting systems. It is a tool used to answer the question of
**84**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
who spent how much for what. Cost centers may be assigned to specific units, staff sections, events, or
functions. Cost centers link MDEPs and Army management structure code with the purpose of the funding.
4-116. Another way to track cost is using a work breakdown structure, which is the process necessary for
subdividing major product deliverables and project work into smaller more manageable components. It serves
as a valuable framework for the technical objectives, and therefore it is product oriented. Its elements should
represent identifiable work products, whether they are equipment, data, or related service products. A work
breakdown structure is a product structure, not an organizational structure, providing the complete definition
of the work to be performed by all participants and the required interfaces between them.

### OBLIGATIONSTAGE

4-117. The obligation stage begins with the activity that creates an obligation in the accounting system of
record. Obligations must be recorded in the official accounting records within 10 calendar days from the day
they are incurred. Several actions may lead to the incurrence of a legal obligation (for example, the acceptance
of a DD Form 448 or the issuance of a travel order). An obligation is a legal (binding) reservation of funds.
Once the obligation is recorded in the accounting system, resource managers ensure the accounting records
will accurately reflect the accrued expenditure/expense and disbursement of payment to the obligation.

### ACCRUEDEXPENDITURES/EXPENSESSTAGE

4-118. Accrued expenditures is a term used for the credits entered into the budgetary accounts to recognize
liabilities incurred for—
•
Services performed by employees, contractors, other government accounting entities, vendors, or
carriers.
•
Goods and other tangible property received.
•
Items such as annuities or insurance claims for which no current service is required.
Accrued expenditures are categorized as either paid or unpaid.
4-119. Expenses are the value of goods or services actually used or consumed during the current period.
Expenses are recognized in the period in which they occur even if payment for them is made in a different
period. Expenses are recorded when incurred (goods are received and services performed even though the
payment of the expense may take place, in whole or part, in another accounting period).
4-120. To illustrate, consider the activities that occur during this stage in terms of a transaction tied to a
contracting action. Following contract award, the vendor performs the requested service or delivers the
requested supplies to the receiving activity. The receiving activity is the unit or activity that first receives the
goods or services. In many instances, the receiving activity may be the same as the activity originating the
request. The receiving activity is responsible for inspecting and accepting the delivered goods or services and
submitting a receiving/acceptance report to the supporting FICO responsible for vendor services. This
acceptance of goods or services represents the accrued expenditure/expense stage in the accounting system.
The receiving report is written evidence that indicates government acceptance of supplies delivered or
services performed and may be submitted electronically in accordance with DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 4.

### DISBURSINGSTAGE

4-121. A disbursement is the payment for goods and services received. Continuing from the previous
scenario, the disbursement stage begins with the vendor submitting an invoice to the billing or paying office
designated in the contract once the goods or services are delivered. The vendor is responsible for providing
the goods or services as specified in the contract. In theater, the FICO serving as the billing or paying office
processes the invoice for payment by reviewing the contract, the receiving/acceptance report, and the invoice
to certify that the payment is appropriate and correct for payment. The FICO then pays the vendor for the
delivered goods or services in accordance with the terms of the contract. Disbursement of the vendor payment
may occur simultaneously along with the delivery of the requested goods or services when employing a FOO
and paying agent to satisfy a local procurement using an SF 44 (U.S. Government Purchase Order – Invoice
– Voucher).
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**85**
**Chapter 4**

## FISCAL RESPONSIBILITY

4-122. The Army has a responsibility to maximize its resources, operate transparently, and execute its budget
according to law and policy. Congress penalizes the Army for losing buying power by cutting funds from its
appropriations. It is important the American people have confidence in the Army’s management of every
taxpayer dollar it receives.

### ANTI-DEFICIENCYACT

4-123. The Antideficiency Act consists of a series of federal statutes that make it illegal for government
officials to spend funding other than as Congress intends. Violations of this act require a formal investigation
process with the results reported to Congress. Violations can lead to adverse personnel actions and even
criminal proceedings. There are three primary legal considerations affecting financial responsibility with
funds entrusted to resource managers and commanders. The first is the “Purpose Statute” (31 USC 1301);
the second legal consideration deals with the time funding is available to spend (31 USC 1502); and the third
legal consideration deals with amount available to spend (31 USC 1517). This is commonly referred to as
“Time, Purpose, and Amount”.

### PURPOSESTATUTE

4-124. The Purpose Statute (31 USC 1341) states that an agency may only spend appropriated funds for a
necessary expense of the programs it is operating. Low-cost items classified as personal items or gifts are not
necessary expenses and may not be purchased with appropriated funds unless there is specific statutory
authority to do so. Resource managers are advised to coordinate with both their higher-level resource
manager and their SJA when questions arise.
4-125. A common pitfall for resource managers and commanders involves violating the “Color of Money”
or the Purpose Statute. This represents a legal violation and is required to be reported to the President via
OMB, Congress, and the Government Accountability Office. Disciplinary action taken must be spelled out
in the report. Congress appropriates funds for specific purposes, with the expectation that those funds will
only be used for those purposes. Exceeding the minor construction threshold or using O&M to purchase
centrally managed equipment are examples of "Color of Money" or Purpose Statute violations.
4-126. Resource managers conduct expense analysis to prevent Purpose Statute violations. They—
•
Verify the expenditure is necessary to accomplish the object of an appropriation or will contribute
materially to the accomplishment of that objective.
•
Assess that the expenditure is not prohibited by law by reviewing the legislation for prohibitive
language and restrictions.
•
Determine if the expenditure is otherwise provided for. If there are two or more appropriations
available for the expenditure, use the most specific appropriation.

### TIME

4-127. Congress grants funding for specific time periods and funds must be obligated during that period. Not
only must funding be obligated during its period of availability, but obligations must be a legitimate need of
that period. Funding must only be obligated to meet a legitimate need arising in the FY for which the
appropriation was made. It restricts this year's appropriated funds from being used to fund next FY's
requirements. Purchasing items with annual funds appropriated for FY 2024 that will be stockpiled and used
in FYs 2025, 2026, and 2027 is a violation of the intent of Congress and is an Antideficiency Act violation.
Contracts may legally be entered into during one FY and cross over into the next. Examples include severable
and non-severable contracts.
4-128. Severable contracts can be separated into components that independently meet a separate need of the
government. Examples of severable service contracts include administrative support, cleaning services, and
trash collection. Statutory authority exists that allows service contracts (and leases) to cross FYs. This is so
an agency can space out their contracts and not have to start all contracts in October. These contracts cannot
exceed 12 months in duration.
4-129. Non-severable contracts produce a single outcome, product, or report that cannot be subdivided for
separate performance. Examples include building renovation, development of a software program, or an
**86**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
effort that will result in an end product. In non-severable service contracts, the government must fund the
entire effort with funding available for obligation at the time the contract is executed. The contract
performance may cross FYs, and the period of performance may exceed 12 months.

### AMOUNT

4-130. Amount represents the final fiscal law principle. You are not allowed to spend more than what is
allotted to your command in the budget. Matching your annual budget to requirements, identifying and
forwarding deficiencies for review, and requesting additional funds helps prevent going over budget.
Periodically match what funds you have remaining with the requirements you have pending. Violating a
provision of the Antideficiency Act means you broke the law. This law cannot be ignored, waived,
interpreted, or reengineered away.
4-131. Status of funds reports will show what is obligated compared to your authority. Just as importantly,
they can show you what is committed that may or may not become an obligation. If you have commitments
that are not obligated and exceed your authority, you will commit an Antideficiency Act violation unless you
cancel them or request and obtain additional funds from higher headquarters prior to incurring the obligation.
There may be a problem if, in any quarterly or annual period:
•
Debit (negative) balances exist in a funding document.
•
Commitments exceed obligations in an account.
•
Commitments exceed authorizations in an account.
•
Obligations exceed authorizations in an account.
•
Obligations fail to be properly recorded.

## TRANSACTION RECONCILIATION

4-132. DOD requires that commitment and obligation transactions recorded in official accounting systems
be reviewed for accuracy, completeness, and timeliness at least three times each FY. The requirement for
reviews of commitments and obligations applies to all appropriations and funds of all DOD appropriations.
This requirement applies not only to direct appropriations, but also to all reimbursable transactions as well
as the Department's revolving and trust funds.

## FINANCIAL STATEMENTS

4-133. Reporting entities issue general purpose financial statements to communicate financial and related
information about the entity. For an entity to be a reporting entity as defined by the Statement of Federal
Financial Accounting Concepts, it would need to meet all of the following criteria: the reporting entity’s
resource manager is responsible for controlling and deploying resources, producing outputs and outcomes,
and executing the budget or a portion thereof (assuming that the entity is included in the budget) and is held
accountable for the entity's performance.
4-134. The preparation of financial statements includes compiling financial records that follow established
governmental accounting principles and procedures into properly formatted reports. Commanders use these
reports to make decisions. Additionally, resource managers create less formal financial statements and reports
to identify errors or transactions that are lagging through the spending chain.

## COST CAPTURING

4-135. Upon being alerted of an impending contingency operation, HQDA will establish a unique special
program code to capture costs. Additionally, the Joint Staff shall issue a three-digit Chairman of the Joint
Chiefs of Staff Project Code, which provides precedence for supply requisitions and facilitates cost capturing
and reporting. Each unit that supports a contingency operation should capture related obligations and
disbursements in its accounting systems at the lowest possible level of the organization. This provides
tracking of obligations and disbursements for affected appropriations. Organizations should not establish cost
accounting systems solely for determining costs for contingency operations but should use the data from
existing FMIS.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**87**
**Chapter 4**
4-136. The level of cost capturing, reporting, and the supported decision maker can also determine whether
an organization is a cost center, segment, or entity. In many cases, the higher headquarters will identify the
reporting entity for the subordinate units.
4-137. Cost reporting is an integral part of the Army's stewardship throughout all levels of RM operations
and must diligently capture and accurately report the cost of contingency operations. The Army is required
to report the obligation of all funds (regardless of source) to cover the incremental costs of a contingency,
including funds received in a supplemental appropriation. HQDA will publish guidance to cover Army-
specific items for cost reporting and validating monthly reporting. The guidance will include information
regarding the cost data source, validation requirements, and variance analysis procedures.

## OBLIGATION MANAGEMENT

4-138. **Obligation management****is a basic function of financial management operations that provides**
**fiscal controls to protect against Antideficiency Act violations.** Tracking obligations enables financial
managers to—
•
Calculate and report available funding.
•
Determine if commitment accounting systems interface issues are occurring.
•
Monitor non-stock orders and payables to determine obligation adjustments.
•
Locate obligations in excess of disbursement and de-obligate the balance for other funding needs or
those that require increase.
•
Identify undelivered or delayed orders.
•
Determine cancellations.
•
Identify patterns in obligation estimates versus actual expenses.
•
Identify increased or decreased obligation performance in programs, Army management structure
codes, or cost centers.
•
Calculate the obligation rate at year-end closeout.
•
Review obligation documents for compliance.
•
Ensure the accounting classification cited on the obligation document is appropriate for the stated
purpose of the obligation.
•
Ensure the amount obligated meets statutory and regulatory provisions.
•
Ensure the obligation is recorded accurately and in a timely manner.
•
Review unliquidated obligations and unobligated commitments on a continual basis.
•
Prepare for mandated formal joint reviews.
4-139. Each MDEP, budget activity group, or functional cost account enables resource managers to monitor
obligations. In some cases, special program codes are designated by the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff
at the operational or strategic level to capture and report costs for specific contingencies, programs, or
exercises. Financial accounts require maintenance to ensure accuracy within the information systems. A key
tool used to perform maintenance on accounts is the Non-Stock Fund Orders and Payables Report. Resource
managers must continuously monitor this report to find obligations in excess of disbursements, duplicated
obligations, negative unliquidated obligations, credits, and accounting or ordering mistakes. Management of
this report assists in the identification of available unused funds.
4-140. Obligations must be recorded no later than 10 calendar days following the day that an obligation is
incurred (to include obligations incurred when invoices are overpaid or duplicate payments are made),
making every effort to record an obligation in the month incurred. DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 3 contains
procedures and policy governing obligation management and should be consulted continuously as changes
to these regulations are common.

### RECORDINGOBLIGATIONS

4-141. When recording obligations—
•
Record obligations in the accounting classification cited on the obligating document. If the
accounting classification is in error, request an amendment or contract modification.
•
Record obligation adjustments for the difference between actual and original amounts.
**88**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
•
Adjust obligations for written changes if they are binding on the contractor without further action.
•
Record obligations providing for a discount at the net price, unless doing so is not cost effective
according to Treasury guidelines. In cases where the servicing accounting system automatically de-
obligates the discount amount upon its realization, it is advisable to record the obligation at the gross
amount.
•
If the contract provides for annual terms with options to extend the terms in subsequent years,
obligate for the amount required to be procured by the USG in the first year. Record the obligation
for each option year when the option is exercised.
•
Adjust obligations to amounts shown on travel authorizations or validated orders when documents
are received.

### ESTIMATEDOBLIGATIONS

4-142. Obligations may be recorded based on estimates. When actual obligation amounts become known,
reverse and replace estimated obligations with actual obligations. The estimates and reversals are
accomplished using DD Form 2406 (Miscellaneous Obligation Document). Serviced activities and
accounting activities will jointly ensure the actual obligation document is received to replace the DD Form
2406.

### OBLIGATIONDOCUMENTATION

4-143. Documentary requirements for recording obligations are provided in 31 USC 1501(a). When
signatures are required to validate obligations, sign the documents before the cited funds expire. Show the
actual date of each signature. When more than one signature is required, record the obligation on the date of
the last required signature.

### EXPIRED/CLOSED/CANCELLEDAPPROPRIATIONS

4-144. Expired appropriations are not available for new obligations. Adjustments may be made to expired
appropriations up to five years, provided the transaction being adjusted occurred prior to the cancellation of
the appropriation. The closure or cancellation of an appropriation does not eliminate the government’s legal
obligation to pay contractors for services rendered or products delivered, nor does it nullify the need for the
accounting activity to maintain an audit trail supporting the existing contingent liability to future
appropriations. Use unexpired funds available for the same general purpose to pay for obligations that would
have been charged to the closed/canceled account if done before account closure/cancellation. See DOD
7000.14-R, Volume 3 for limitations and additional guidance.

### DORMANTACCOUNTREVIEW-QUARTERLY

4-145. The Dormant Account Review-Quarterly replaces the former Triannual Review and is effective for
all DOD Components. This change exemplifies DOD commitment to effective stewardship of taxpayer
dollars through improved execution of budgetary resources. It also reflects DOD continuous improvement
efforts and maturation of internal controls while enabling senior leadership to provide oversight and defend
budgets. The Dormant Account Review-Quarterly serves as a quality control mechanism of entity-level
internal control activities. In addition to providing USD(C) oversight, this review improves DOD's ability to
execute all available appropriations before expiration and ensures remaining open obligations are valid and
support accurate financial and budgetary reporting.
4-146. The quarterly review tracks the dormancy and validity of DOD component obligations and ensures
proper actions are being taken to correct or expedite the timely execution and full utilization of appropriated
funds. 31 USC 1554(c) requires establishing internal controls to ensure an adequate review of obligated
balances to support the year-end certification required by law. DOD requires that commitment and obligation
transactions recorded in official accounting systems be reviewed quarterly for accuracy, completeness, and
timeliness as part of the Dormant Activity Report (see DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 3 for more details).

### SECTION VII - INTERNAL CONTROLS

4-149. Internal controls are the rules, procedures, techniques, and devices employed by finance commanders
to mitigate conflicts of interest, fraud, and risk. While audits aim to provide an external and independent
review, internal controls are an organization's self-evaluation. Commanders establish internal controls and
periodic reviews to identify flaws and deficiencies in daily business to reduce the risk of potential loss of
funds.
4-150. A common misconception exists that internal controls require less attention during contingency
operations. Intense congressional scrutiny of contracting and procurement operations in previous operations,
as well as media accounts documenting lapses of internal control in both theaters, demonstrate the significant
importance internal controls play in Army operations not only in peacetime, but also during contingency
operations. Internal controls should be in place for high-risk areas such as cash, fuel, ammo, and rations to
prevent fraud, waste, and abuse. Without proper internal controls, commanders are placing their units and
mission accomplishment at risk. This section covers the essential elements of the internal control process.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**91**
**Chapter 4**
4-151. Internal controls must be embedded in everything a finance unit does and must be a priority for every
commander, leader, and Soldier. Internal controls are not simply an adherence to a series of checklists; they
are a continuous process that ensures the effective and efficient use of entrusted financial resources in mission
execution across all areas of operations. Continuous development and review of internal controls ensures that
what should happen in daily operations does happen.

### Internal Controls – Financial Instability in Niger

In the summer of 2023, hostilities in Niger escalated, leading the US Treasury to
remove Nigerien banks as trusted financial institutions. Consequently, the U.S. could
no longer make EFT payments for vendors supporting U.S. forces in the region,
causing an accumulation of unpaid invoices. All efforts to make payments within Niger
failed. This resulted in the Special Operations Command Africa requesting assistance
from the 106th Corps Finance Battalion for payment support.
While the 106th prepared to deploy an FST to make cash payments, the internal control
team worked with Special Operations Command Africa, contracting, and DFAS to
establish a common operating picture of the contracts requiring cash payments. The
internal control team’s effort was critical to the prevention of duplicate or erroneous
payments since some payments had already been processed to vendor bank accounts
outside of Niger. The team ensured proper finance procedures were in place to prevent
any duplicate payments by the FST.
The 106th's FST departed Ramstein Air Base for Niger with $1.8M U.S. dollars. Over
the course of the week, the FST paid over $1.3M U.S. dollars to vendors providing
critical support to U.S. Forces in Niger. The diligent work of the internal control team
identified discrepancies and errors in the documentation for the remaining $500K worth
of unpaid invoices. Instead of proceeding with these contract payments, they returned
the documentation for correction to ensure the integrity of the financial process. The
FST followed internal control procedures, using thoroughly recorded supporting
documents, to track the status of funds and prevent incorrect payments.

## KEY INTERNAL CONTROLS

4-152. Key internal controls are those controls that are absolutely essential to ensuring that critical processes
operate as intended and that resources are safeguarded from fraud, waste, and misuse. HQDA functional
proponents are responsible for identifying key management controls based on risk. Some of these controls
are—
•
Delegation of authority and certifying officers.

•
Commitment approval - fund approval.
•
Obligation approval.
•
Goods/services receipt approval.
•
Defense Travel System Insufficient Funding Error Message.
•
Time and attendance certification.

### FUNDSCONTROL

4-153. Distribution of funds (31 USC 1514) requires agencies to prescribe by regulation a system of
administrative control not inconsistent with accounting procedures prescribed by law. The Program and
Budget Accounting System is the vehicle for controlling receipt and distribution of funds within the Army.
This includes all formal fund distributions at HQDA, operating agency, and installation levels and informal
distribution made by operating agencies under the Fund Allowance System. The Program and Budget
Accounting System issues both fund authorization documents and Fund Allowance System documents.
Distribution of targets or allowances to installation activity directors, satellites, and other activities which are
made outside of the Program and Budget Accounting System must be documented and delegated in writing.
4-154. Fund control is the authority exercised over the receipt, distribution, use, and management of budget
authority appropriated to, or administered by, the Army to ensure that—
•
Funds are used only for authorized purposes.
•
Funds are used economically and efficiently.
•
Distributions, commitments, obligations, and expenditures do not exceed amounts authorized and
available.
•
Distribution, obligation, or disbursement of funds is not reserved or otherwise withheld without
congressional knowledge and approval. This does not preclude deferral or withholding action to
provide reserves for contingencies or reprogramming within delegated authority.
4-155. Fund control systems and administrative procedures will—
•
Provide for segregation of duties in the fund distribution, obligational, and disbursement process.
•
Require clear delegation of all funding authority in writing to lower echelon officials.
•
Perpetuate administrative and statutory limitations established by higher authority.
•
Distribute, monitor, and control funds at the highest level possible.
•
Establish appropriate reserves or other withholding of funds for contingencies.
•
Ensure that fund withdrawals are appropriately coordinated before being made.
•
Control funded reimbursement authority and automatic reimbursable authority on funding
documents.
•
Provide a mechanism to update amounts available. This includes preventing and detecting over-
distribution, over-commitment, over-obligation, and over-disbursement of funds at appropriate
levels.
•
Accountants involved in budget, finance, and accounting will be familiar with the provisions of
DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 14, and the Anti-deficiency Act (31 USC 1517) and how they relate to
informal and formal subdivision of funds.
4-156. The status of funds is determined by using a variety of FMIS reports generated to assist in determining
funding balance, commitments, obligations, and disbursements. Resource managers provide timely, accurate,
relevant, and reliable financial information that enables leaders and managers to incorporate cost
considerations into their decision-making process.

### MAINTAININGSUPPORTINGDOCUMENTATION

4-157. Title 44 USC Section 3301 and the National Archives and Records Administration General Record
Schedules include document retention requirements applicable to federal entities. The DOD has also
developed supplementary guidance in DODI 5015.02 and DOD 7000.14-R, Volume 1. However, these
requirements do not emphasize the retention requirements of documents necessary to assert and support audit
readiness.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**93**
**Chapter 4**
4-158. Resource managers must adhere to professional standards, which have been codified as Auditing
Standards. These standards do not directly contain document retention requirements. Instead, they define
evidential matter (for example, supporting documentation) that auditors must obtain and test to form an
opinion on the reporting entity's financial statements.
4-159. Accordingly, the document retention requirements to achieve auditability and reliable financial
information are sometimes different and more stringent (longer duration) than the requirements set forth by
the National Archives and Records Administration General Record Schedules, DOD directives, and reporting
entity-specific requirements. The retention requirements for auditability may be less stringent in some cases;
therefore, reporting entities must adhere to the most stringent record retention requirement.

## SEPARATION OF DUTIES

4-160. Separation of duties creates a situation that shall preclude errors or attempts at fraud or embezzlement
from going undetected. Key duties such as authorizing, approving, and recording transactions; issuing or
receiving assets; making payments; preparing checks and check signing; certification of funding; and
reviewing or auditing shall be assigned to separate individuals to minimize the risk of loss to the government
to the greatest extent possible.
4-161. The disbursing officer will not be assigned duties that create a potential conflict of interest. For
example: A payment is made under the disbursing officer’s DSSN that results in a request for a waiver of
indebtedness. If the disbursing officer also supervises those individuals involved in the adjudication of
requests for waivers, then the disbursing officer is placed in a position of influence over the decision of the
waiver.
4-162. Appropriate separation of duties is not always practical or possible due to time constraints, manpower
shortages, or the use of automated systems. For example, payments and collections through the
Intragovernmental Payment and Collection system can occur outside the disbursing work center without
weakening internal controls because the payee is always another federal agency and recovery of an erroneous
payment is assured. Other situations can require closer scrutiny. The commander and the disbursing officer
shall be aware of situations where valid, long-standing separation of duties cannot be achieved, recognize
that internal controls have been weakened as a result, and make every effort to compensate for loss of the
internal control. Unique situations resulting in an inability to separate appropriate responsibilities shall be
reported to the applicable DFAS or Service commander, together with a request for a waiver and
recommendations for compensating for the inability to separate roles.

### SECTION VIII - FINANCIAL DATA ANALYTICS

4-163. To optimize the benefits of the integrated ERP environment (GFEBS and GCSS-A) and the large
amounts of data they produce, the Army must mature its approaches to financial data analytics. Resource
managers must move from descriptive and diagnostic analytics that are historically based, predominantly
transactional, and focused on performance evaluation toward enabling predictive and prescriptive analytics
using real-time data.
4-164. Leveraging big data and executing enhanced financial data analytics capabilities is an FM strategic
initiative. The initiative goal is to turn financial data available in theater into business insight, facilitating
more informed decisions and driving resource efficiencies for the commanders operating in theater.
4-165. Financial data analytics is an emerging capability that optimizes business processes, enables
conservation, and safeguards financial resources. Due to constrained resources and audit readiness
requirements, financial data analytics is a skill and a capability that will provide commanders at every
echelon, both home station and deployed, a tailorable, integrated, and scientific financial readiness picture
across the full spectrum of operations, even within a joint, interagency, or multinational environment.
Financial data analytics can leverage non-financial aspects of the OE (for example, HN holiday periods where
vendors may be unavailable) to identify trends, schedule challenges, and other cultural aspects that may have
an impact on day-to-day operations or the command spending priorities.
4-166. Financial data analytics synchronizes spending plan development, cost management, and decision
support using ERP automated systems and tools such as GFEBS, GCSS-Army, and business analytical tools
**94**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**
**Resource Management**
to align resource requirements with commanders’ lines of effort. Cost management collects and links
financial (cost) data with non-financial output and performance data, presenting the information in a way
directly related to major mission objectives.
4-167. Resource managers that leverage financial data ensure commanders are aware of existing resource
implications in order for them to make resource-informed decisions and obtain the necessary funding that
allows them to accomplish their mission.

### Data Analytics – Talisman Sabre 23

The USARPAC FM team utilized the data framework of manage, analyze, visualize,
and present to extract and automate data from multiple systems of record, seamlessly
integrating data into operational processes during Talisman Saber 23. Leveraging user
data input for emerging requirements in a standardized data entry allowed, for the first
time, a dynamic fiscal landscape with known and emerging requirements for funding
consideration. The publishing of an interactive dashboard allowed each member of the
fiscal triad to fight from a common data picture and significantly reduced the time from
validation of requirement to disbursement. This effort enabled near-real-time data that
enhanced operational agility and set the tone for all future campaigning efforts.
Commanders, staff, and partnered nations were able to obtain data directly in an
operationalized format and make informed decisions.
Furthermore, the careful consideration of cost collection from the G-8 team on how
costs were captured using attributes and standardization enabled quick analysis during
the exercise which enables prescriptive analytics. This standardization of data also
enhances predictive analytics as it allows for the utilization of artificial intelligence tools
to project future exercise costs. By operationalizing data, the USARPAC FM team was
able to enhance decision-making, improve efficiency, and set the stage for future
success.

## COST CAPTURING

4-168. Financial data analytics and business intelligence provide commanders with actionable information
about key performance indicators, along with supporting financial and budget strategies that influence critical
funding and contracting decisions during contingency operations. Financial data can be mined and leveraged
to provide a complete and comprehensive cost analysis and to support achieving and sustaining auditability,
all while funding commander requirements efficiently and effectively. Resource managers have the
responsibility to improve financial readiness and provide Soldiers and commanders with the business analytic
support for resource-informed decision-making.

## ECONOMIC ANALYSIS

4-169. An **economic analysis****is a detailed report identifying all the financial aspects of military spending**
**of a specific geographical area and the economic effect of the United States forces’ presence**. It enables
commanders to assess the value proposition of each requirement through its life cycle (concept, testing,
production, operations and support, and disposal) to fulfill the Army's strategic goals and support resource-
informed decision-making processes.
4-170. Economic analysis is a preferred action that provides financial information to organizational decision
makers. It defines a solution aimed at achieving specific Army and organizational objectives by quantifying
the potential financial impacts and other business benefits such as—
•
Savings and cost avoidance.
•
Revenue enhancements and cash-flow improvements.
•
Fund performance improvements.
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**95**
**Chapter 4**
4-171. The economic analysis process considers non-financial or non-quantifiable benefits of a specific
course of action (COA) within the OE. This feature is important because although the financial data may
favor one COA over another, there may be situations where the non-financial data/information is considered
more important to the analyst or senior decision maker. Furthermore, the non-financial criteria and
conclusions may support something other than what the financial data favors.
4-172. This process also provides an evaluation and justification of a proposed solution (including any
associated expenditures) before a significant amount of funds are invested. Economic analysis documents the
reasons for the spending and the options available and describes how the expense helps the organization
reach its goals. It guides the decision maker to focus on the major issues surrounding the recommended
solution and to not spend time on minor issues.
**96**
**FM 4-80**
**21 April 2025**

### Chapter 5

## Financial Management Readiness

This chapter covers the importance of FM readiness through planning, plans and orders, and
training. FM support in theater requires mission readiness of a unique set of skills and
capabilities critical to sustainment operations. As the fourth element of the sustainment
warfighting function, FM integration during all phases of operations is critical to the successful
deployment of finance units.

## FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT PLANNING CONSIDERATIONS

5-1. FM planners coordinate through a network of explicit command and staff relationships and implicit
coordination between staffs that exist from the ASCC down to the lowest tactical finance unit. In between
these levels of command, various finance units and coordinating staffs within multiple organizations (for
example, TSC, FISC, ESC, division, sustainment brigade, and FIBN) integrate, synchronize, and plan theater
FM operations. G-8 staffs at corps echelon and above advise and resource their commander's priorities while
the TSC's FIOC or FISC, in coordination with the theater Army G-8, plans theater finance operations
executed by tactical-level finance units. From the homeland, USAFMCOM plans critical strategic FM
support to operations in theater. Active collaborative planning ensures an ongoing analysis of commanders’
tasks and priorities to ensure that adequate and proper financial resources are available and applied to aid
success.
5-2. Planners must attend and participate in planning cells and working groups in order to anticipate
requirements and build comprehensive finance sustainment solutions. The expertise provided by the FM staff
assists the commander in accurately visualizing an operation to make appropriate decisions. Functional cells
plan according to time horizon and assist with C2. Integrating processes include intelligence preparation of
the battlefield, information collection, targeting, risk management, and knowledge management. See FM 6-0
for more information on staff organization and meetings.

### FINANCIALMANAGEMENTPLANNINGPRIORITIES

5-3. During large-scale combat operations in a multidomain environment, degraded communications can be
generated by cyber intrusion, lack of power, or other events. Adversaries will attempt to disrupt, degrade, or
curtail communications and access to enterprise systems. In response to this threat, finance units must train
and prepare to operate in a disconnected OE with redundant manual systems. Sustainment operations rely on
effective ERP systems, which require access to the DOD Information Network. Planners must place emphasis
on coordination efforts with G-6 elements to gain access to necessary networks. Proper training and planning
allow FM units to operate in denied, degraded, and disrupted communications environments. Some
considerations include the following:
•
Secure low bandwidth in sustainment tactical network environment.
•
Maintain manual input proficiency.
•
Maintain and complete manual forms.
•
Perform manual disbursing operations (non-electronic commerce capability).
•
Conduct record keeping and supporting documentation management (hard copy).
•
Maintain manual accountability.
5-4. Communications are critical for FM units to provide timely and critical support. FMIS and FM
capabilities depend greatly on the ability of finance units to communicate throughout the area of operations.
Widely dispersed units must rely on communications with their headquarters to accomplish their mission.
Although units use wire communications, they also need to possess and be able to utilize mobile and secure
communications equipment. They must be able reach-back from forward positions in brigade areas, through
division, corps or other forward areas in theater.
5-5. The tempo and lethality of large-scale combat operations may overwhelm FM capabilities; therefore,
planners will continue to monitor fragmentary orders and conduct a detailed analysis and assessment of
**21 April 2025**
**FM 4-80**
**97**
**Chapter 5**
support requirements and availability of resources based on the OE and mission tempo. Additionally,
planning should consider changes to operational variables to include the limited availability of commercial
support due to increased enemy activity.
5-6. Focus during conflict should remain on maintaining fiscal stewardship and auditability at all times,
despite the rapid changes and unexpected transitions between phases of operations or movement and
maneuver between areas of support.
5-7. Planners must anticipate and plan for the employment of FM capabilities and proactively gather
emerging information to update running estimates. An increase in enemy finance networking during large-
scale combat is expected; therefore, support should be flexible and tailorable in support of tactical
commanders, never bypassing internal controls or fiscal stewardship responsibilities. During conflict,
running estimates should capture the following FM considerations:
•
Facts relating to availability of commercial support, changes in HN banking infrastructure and
theater policy, access to enterprise systems, status of contingency DSSNs, ecommerce, LDAs and
replenishment of U.S. and foreign currency.
•
Assumptions relating to forecasts of cash and currency requirements, changes to finance force
structure, degradation in HN banking capabilities, and potential increase in enemy finance networks.
•
Friendly force status (higher, lower, and adjacent units).
•
Conclusions and recommendations for future FM operations and COAs.

### FISCALDIPLOMACY

5-8. Fiscal diplomacy involves the Army's ability to procure and pay for required supplies and services
through the coordinated efforts of contracting, RM, finance operations, unified action partners, and the U.S.
State Department, with a holistic understanding of the culture of the personnel providing the support. To
effectively manage U.S. relationships with foreign governments, international organizations, and other
countries, commanders should consider the importance of fiscal diplomacy to effectively execute stability
tasks requiring the purchase of supplies and services from local vendors or contractors. Prompt or delayed
payment is an important aspect of fiscal diplomacy and can enhance or impede relationships.
5-9. HN support to economic development includes the Army FM enterprise, which uses economic analysis
to identify specific country or region procurement methods that may create delayed payments that may cause
mistrust from the local vendors. FM capability may include local currency, other applicable foreign currency,
U.S. dollars, and possibly precious metals to establish trust and goodwill with local vendors. At times
payment can occur through EFT, credit card, or cash. While there is a desire to focus solely on EFT, this may
not be the most effective payment method. Determining the best mix of options to ensure timely payments
bolsters credibility. Fiscal diplomacy involves identifying the most effective payment method to ensure we
do not inadvertently create ill will or foster hostility, regardless of current trends and desires. There are times
when cash is the best option.

### RULES OFALLOCATION FORFINANCE

5-10. Planners employ finance assets and personnel across theater by following planning factors in
determining the minimum number of finance units required to provide the necessary support within an AOR:
•
FISC: One per TSC or ESC.
•
T-FIBN: One per 2 to 6 T-FICOs.
•
C-FIBN: One per 2 to 6 C-FICOs.
•
T-FICO:
▪
One per ESC.
▪
Two per sustainment brigade in the joint security area.
▪
One per contracting support brigade.
▪
Two platoons per T-FICO.
▪
Four FSTs per platoon.
•
C-FICO:
▪
One per corps.
▪
One per division.
▪
Two platoons per C-FICO.
▪
Four FSTs per platoon.
